Testimony of Mortgagees: Existing Pledge vs. Destroyed Pledge
Ruling and reasoning on mortgagee testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the admissibility of mortgagees' testimony regarding the pledge and explain the underlying reasoning (maghnam/maghram).

Core Idea
Testimony of mortgagees is accepted when the pledge exists (no gain) but rejected when destroyed (repels loss).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq explains a nuanced rule of testimony (shahādah) specific to mortgagees (murtahinūn). The ruling turns on whether the pledged property is still present or has been destroyed. Understanding the rationale helps preserve the integrity of judicial testimony by eliminating self-interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses two distinct situations: (1) the pledged property (rahn) is still extant (qā’im), and (2) the pledged property has been destroyed (hālik). List them separately.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
When the pledge exists, the testimony of the two mortgagees is accepted (tuqbal). When it is destroyed, their testimony is rejected (lā tuqbal).
3
Analyze the reasoning – maghnam and maghram
The criterion is whether the testimony brings gain (maghnam) or repels loss (maghram). If the pledge exists, affirming it does not bring gain; rather, it imposes liability (they could be held responsible for loss). If the pledge is destroyed, testifying that it was a pledge would repel loss (they avoid losing their debt). Therefore, testimony is accepted only when there is no self-interest.
The Arabic terms: مغنم (gain) and مغرم (loss/harm).
4
Apply the reasoning to confirm the ruling
Explain why the second scenario creates suspicion: if the pledge is destroyed, the debt is discharged in the apparent sense; testifying that it was a pledge would re-establish the debt, benefiting the mortgagees. Hence, their testimony is tainted by self-interest and rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو شهد المرتهنان تقبل لأنهما لا يجران إلى أنفسهما مغنماً ولا يدفعان مغرماً بل يضران بأنفسهما متى كان الرهن قائماً، وإن كان هالكاً لا تقبل شهادتهما لأنهما يمنعان عن أنفسهما مغرماً لأن بهلاك الرهن سقط الدين وبرئ الراهن عن الدين ظاهراً، ومتى قبلت شهادتهما لم يصح الرهن فلا يسقط حقهما
And if the two mortgagees testify, it is accepted because they do not draw gain to themselves nor repel loss; rather, they harm themselves when the pledge exists. But if it is destroyed, their testimony is not accepted because they repel loss from themselves, since with the destruction of the pledge the debt is discharged and the mortgagor is released from the debt apparently, and if their testimony is accepted the pledge would not be valid, so their right would not be discharged.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage discusses the testimony of the mortgagees (creditors) regarding the existence of a pledge. The ruling depends on the status of the pledged property. If the pledged property still exists (is present and not destroyed), the mortgagees' testimony that it is a pledge is accepted. The reason: by affirming the pledge, they are not gaining any advantage; rather, they are potentially harming themselves because the existence of the pledge means the mortgagee has liability (if the property is lost through his negligence, he must compensate). They are also not repelling any loss from themselves. Therefore, their testimony is free from suspicion. However, if the pledged property is destroyed (e.g., lost or perished), the mortgagees' testimony that it was a pledge is not accepted. The reason: if the property is destroyed, the debt is considered discharged (the mortgagee loses his security) and the mortgagor is freed from the debt in the apparent sense (if the mortgagee cannot prove otherwise). By testifying that it was a pledge, the mortgagees would be trying to establish the pledge, which would mean that the debt is still secured and the mortgagee's right continues. This would prevent the mortgagees from suffering a loss (the loss of their debt). Since they have a self-interest in avoiding loss, their testimony is rejected. In other words, if the property exists, they have no motive to lie; if destroyed, they have a motive to protect their claim.

Key Terms
مغنم (maghnam): Gain or profit; here, the mortgagees do not gain by testifying.
مغرم (maghram): Loss or harm; mortgagees would repel loss by testifying if the pledge is destroyed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is the testimony of the mortgagees that the property is a pledge accepted?
A) Always
B) Only when the pledged property exists
C) Only when the pledged property is destroyed
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The admissibility of mortgagee testimony depends on whether the pledge exists: if it does, they have no motive to lie; if destroyed, they do.

Testimony of Creditors in a Condition
Ruling on testimony of two creditors with a conditional pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of creditors in a conditional pledge and explain the distinction based on their intent.

Core Idea
Testimony that harms one's own interest without gain is accepted; that which seeks personal benefit is rejected.

This lesson examines a specific scenario where two creditors testify that the pledged slave belongs to another, but they waive their right to the pledge. The Hanafi school accepts such testimony because it harms their own position without benefiting them. However, if they demand something that serves their interest, their testimony is rejected. We will explore the legal reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The testimony of two creditors. Determine the ruling in each scenario: accepted when they waive, rejected when they demand.
2
Locate the 'illa
What is the effective cause that differentiates the two scenarios? It is the creditors' intent: waiving their right vs. demanding something for themselves.
3
Analyze the talil
The text states 'because they testify against themselves... and do not seek to revoke.' Understand how this justifies acceptance in the first scenario.
The witnesses are harming their own interest without any gain.
4
Apply the principle to the opposing scenario
When they demand something, why is it rejected? Because they testify for themselves and seek to revoke the contract, which benefits them.
5
Ensure consistency with broader Hanafi principles
The ruling aligns with the general Hanafi principle that testimony is not accepted when it directly benefits the witness, unless it harms their own interest without gain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باع رجلان متاعاً بألف درهم من رجل على أن يرهنهما عبداً بعينه ثم شهد أن العبد لرجل وقالا نرضى أن يكون ديناً بالرهن تقبل شهادتهما لأنهما يشهدان على أنفسها بإبطال حقهما في الحبس ولا يجران إلى أنفسهما مغنماً ولا يدفعان مغرماً ولا يسعيان في نقض عقد، ولو طلبا لا تقبل لأنهما يشهدان لأنفسهما برهن ويسعيان في نقض عقد تم بينهما وليس لهما النقض
Two men sold goods for a thousand dirhams to a man on condition that he pledge a specific slave to them. Then they testify that the slave belongs to another man, and they say, 'We are satisfied that it be a debt with the pledge.' Their testimony is accepted because they testify against themselves by invalidating their right of retention, and they do not draw gain to themselves nor repel loss, nor do they seek to revoke a contract. But if they demand (something else), it is not accepted because they testify for themselves for a pledge and seek to revoke a contract that was concluded between them, and they have no right to revoke.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage presents a scenario involving two creditors who sold goods on condition that the buyer pledge a specific slave as security. Later, the creditors testify that the slave actually belongs to someone else, but they state they are willing to accept the debt without the pledge (i.e., they waive their right of retention). The ruling: their testimony is accepted because by testifying that the slave is not the debtor's property, they are essentially invalidating their own right to hold the slave as a pledge. This harms their own interest (they lose the security) and benefits no one. They are not seeking to gain anything or avoid loss; they are simply acknowledging the reality. Moreover, they are not trying to revoke the original contract of sale; they are only affecting the pledge. However, if they demand something else (likely meaning if they insist on their right to the pledge despite the testimony, or if they try to use the testimony to their advantage), then their testimony is not accepted. This is because in that case, they would be testifying for their own benefit (to establish a pledge) and seeking to disrupt the existing contract. The distinction is based on the presence or absence of personal interest.

Key Terms
الحبس (al-habs): The right of the pledgee to retain the pledged property until the debt is paid.
نقض العقد (naqd al-'aqd): Revocation of a contract; the creditors are not trying to revoke the sale, only the pledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When do the two creditors' testimony that the pledged slave belongs to another become accepted?
A) When they demand their right to the pledge
B) When they declare they are satisfied with the debt without the pledge
C) Always, because they are witnesses
D) Never, because they are interested parties

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of testimony from interested parties in Hanafi fiqh hinges on whether the testimony harms the witness's own interest without personal gain.

Two Claimants Each Claiming the Same Pledge: Rulings During Lifetime
Rulings on competing claims of pledge from the same person during his lifetime

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants each assert a pledge on the same property from the same debtor, distinguishing between cases based on possession and dating, and explain when istihsan overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
When evidence is equal, Hanafi istihsan gives each claimant half of his claimed right rather than rejecting both.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, we examine a complex scenario where two creditors both claim that the same property is pledged to them by the same debtor. The ruling depends on who holds the property and whether the claims are dated. We will see how possession strengthens evidence and how, when evidence is balanced, Hanafi law uses istihsan to achieve a fair outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
Classify the case by possession (one claimant has the pledged property, both have it, or neither) and by whether the claims are dated.
These factors determine the ruling.
2
Understand the role of possession
If one claimant possesses the property and neither claim has a date, the possessor wins because possession indicates prior right.
Similar to two claimants of purchase, where the one holding the goods prevails.
3
Apply the date rule
If both claimants have dates on their evidence, the earlier date prevails; the later evidence is rejected as invalid.
Time stamps create a hierarchy of proof.
4
Analyze the undated, no-possession case
If neither has possession and no dates, strict analogy (qiyas) gives no judgment. But istihsan (juristic preference) gives each claimant half of his claimed right.
Hanafi law prioritizes equity when formal proof fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادعيا على رجل أن كل واحد له الرهن فهي على قسمين، أما إذا كان الرهن في يد أحدهما أو في أيديهما أو في يد الراهن والدعوى منهما حال حياة الراهن أو بعد وفاته وقد أرخا ذلك كله أو لم يؤرخا فإن كان الرهن في يد أحدهما ولم يؤرخا فهو أولى لأنه قد ترجحت بينة ذي اليد باليد لأن يده تدل على أنه سبق ارتهانه ولأن يده صحيحة من حيث الظاهر فلا يجوز نقضها إلا أن يعلم بطلاتها كما لو ادعيا الشراء من واحد والمبيع في يد ا، فإن أرخا يقضى لأسبقهما تاريخاً لأن البينة التي آخرهما تاريخاً غير مقبولة لأنها قامت فاسد وكان الذي هو أسبق انفرد بإقامة البينة، وإن لم يؤرخا لا يقضى لهما قياساً وفي الاستحسان لكل واحد نصفه بنصف حقه لأن رهن كل واحد منهما نبذ بينتهما معاً فصح الرهن فصار العبد محبوساً بحق كل واحد منهما على الكمال. هذا كله في حال حياة الراهن
If two men each claim that they have a pledge against a man, this is of two types. If the pledged property is in the hand of one of them, or in both their hands, or in the hand of the mortgagor, and the claim is made during the lifetime of the mortgagor or after his death, and they have dated it or not dated it. If the pledge is in the hand of one of them and they have not dated it, then he is given precedence because the evidence of the one in possession is strengthened by possession, since his hand indicates that his pledge preceded, and because his possession is valid apparently, so it cannot be invalidated unless its invalidity is known, just as if two claim purchase from one and the goods are in the hand of one. If they have dated it, judgment is given to the one with the earlier date, because the evidence with the later date is not accepted because it stands invalid, and the earlier one alone presented evidence. If they have not dated it, judgment is not given to either by qiyas, but by istihsan each gets half of his right, because the pledge of each one... their evidence together, so the pledge is valid, and the slave becomes detained for the right of each fully. All this is during the lifetime of the mortgagor.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a lengthy discussion on competing claims to a pledge on the same property from the same debtor. It outlines the rules during the debtor's lifetime. The key factors are possession and historical dating. If one of the claimants has actual possession of the pledged property, and neither claim has a specific date, the possessor gets priority. This is because possession is a strong indicator of prior right, analogous to a case where two people claim to have bought from the same seller and one has the goods. If both have dates on their evidence, the earlier date prevails. If neither has dates, then by strict analogy (qiyas) no judgment is given, but by istihsan (juristic preference), each claimant is entitled to half of his claimed right (i.e., the property is considered a pledge for both, and each has a share proportionate to his claim, and the property is held as security for both debts). The reasoning is that both pieces of evidence are valid and both pledges are established concurrently, so the property is detained for both claims in full (the amount owed to each), but practically each gets half because the property may be sold and proceeds distributed proportionally.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from strict analogy for reasons of equity or convenience.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here it yields no judgment, but istihsan overrides.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to istihsan, what is the ruling when two claimants each assert a pledge on the same property from the same debtor, without dates or possession?
A) Both claims are rejected
B) The property is divided equally between them
C) Each gets half of his claimed right
D) The property is given to the debtor

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when evidence for competing pledges is equal and no possession or date resolves priority, istihsan allows each claimant half of his claimed right, preserving fairness.

Competing Pledge Claims After Death: Abu Yusuf vs. the Two
Ruling on competing pledge claims after debtor's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on joint pledge after death and evaluate Abu Yusuf's qiyas-based objection.

Core Idea
After death, a joint pledge is valid for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf invalidates it because two cannot share possession.

After the death of a debtor, if two creditors each claim a pledge over the same property, how should the law treat their competing claims? The Hanafi school is divided: the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) allow each to take half as pledge by istihsan, while Abu Yusuf rejects this entirely using qiyas. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind both positions and clarifies the practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggering scenario
Recognize that the rule under discussion applies after the death of the mortgagor (raahin), not during his lifetime. Two claimants each prove a pledge over the same property.
During lifetime, the rule is already established; the dispute is about the posthumous case.
2
State the majority position (the Two)
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule that each claimant gets half of the property as pledge for his debt. The property is sold, each receives his due, and any surplus goes to general creditors.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Present Abu Yusuf's dissenting view and his proof
Abu Yusuf says no pledge is granted; all creditors (including these two) share equally. His proof: granting a joint pledge is like granting a pledge over an undivided share (mushā‘), which is invalid because two cannot have exclusive possession (yad and ḥabs) simultaneously.
He applies qiyas: just as a joint pledge is invalid during life, it must be invalid after death.
4
Analyze the difference in objectives
The majority counters: during life, the objective of pledge is possession and detention; after death, the objective shifts to securing the debt as a means. Since possession is no longer required, the impediment of joint possession disappears.
The text says: 'the objective is sought by its ruling, not by its specific object'.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the majority view, the two claimants have priority over general creditors; under Abu Yusuf's view, they share equally with all creditors. The legal effect directly impacts who gets paid first.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the practical outcome of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا كله في حال حياة الراهن، فأما بعد وفاته لو أقام كل واحد البينة على ارتهانه منه يقضى لكل واحد بنصفه رهناً بنصف حقه يباع فيه عندهما وما بقي للغرماء. وقال أبو يوسف : لا يقضى لهما بشيء وهو قول الغرماء بالحصص قياساً لأن القضاء بالرهن منهما قضاء برهن مشاع وأنه باطل كما في حالة الحياة لهما أن القصد مطلوب بحكمه لا بعينه لأنه شرع ليكون وسيلة وذريعة إلى حكمه وحكمه الرهن بعد الموت في حق هذا الحكم بخلاف حال الحياة لأن ثمة المقصود من الرهن هو ملك اليد والحبس ولا يملك اثنان اليد والحبس في المشاع دائماً فلا يمكن القضاء بالرهن
All this during the lifetime of the mortgagor. As for after his death, if each one presents evidence of his pledge from him, judgment is given to each for half of his right as a pledge, to be sold for it according to the two, and what remains is for the creditors. Abu Yusuf said: No judgment is given to them for anything, and it is the statement of the creditors by shares, by qiyas, because judgment of the pledge from them is a judgment of a joint pledge, and that is invalid just as during life. For them, the objective is sought by its ruling, not by its specific object, because it is legislated as a means and a path to its ruling, and its ruling is that the pledge after death in respect of this ruling is different from during life, because there the objective of the pledge is ownership of hand and detention, and two cannot own hand and detention in joint property permanently, so judgment of the pledge is not possible.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario after the death of the mortgagor (debtor). The default position (attributed to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) is that if two claimants each present evidence that the deceased pledged the same property to them, judgment is given to each for half of his claimed right as a pledge. That is, the property is considered a pledge for both debts proportionally, and it is sold to satisfy both claims; any surplus after paying both goes to the general creditors of the estate. This follows the istihsan logic from the lifetime case. However, Abu Yusuf disagrees. He argues that no judgment should be given for either pledge. His reasoning: granting a judgment of pledge would create a joint (mush') pledge over the property, which is invalid (because a pledge requires exclusive possession by the pledgee, and joint possession is not possible). During the debtor's lifetime, the objective of pledge is to give the pledgee the right to retain (yad and habs), and two people cannot jointly have that right in an undivided share. After death, the objective changes: the pledge serves as a means to secure the debt, but the essential element of possession (yad) cannot be shared. Therefore, by analogy (qiyas), the pledge is invalid, and the creditors should share in the estate as unsecured creditors. Abu Yusuf's view is that the two claimants have no priority over other creditors. The text mentions that his opinion is 'the statement of the creditors by shares' meaning they all share equally.

Key Terms
مشاع (musha'): Joint or undivided share; Abu Yusuf argues that a pledge cannot be valid over an undivided share because possession cannot be shared.
الغرماء (al-ghuramā'): General creditors; those who have unsecured claims against the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key reason Abu Yusuf gives for not granting any pledge after the debtor's death?
A) The evidence is weak
B) A joint pledge is invalid because two cannot share possession
C) The debt is not proven
D) The creditors have no claim

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, after the debtor's death, competing pledge claims are resolved by granting each claimant half of the property as pledge for half his debt, based on istihsan; Abu Yusuf's alternative view based on strict qiyas is not the operative position.

Competing Claims of Pledge from Two Different Mortgagors
Rules when each claimant claims a pledge from a different person on the same property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which mortgagee is preferred when two claimants each allege a pledge from different owners on the same property, by analysing the presence or absence of the mortgagors in court.

Core Idea
When both mortgagors are present, the outsider mortgagee prevails over the one in possession.

This passage examines a procedural nuance in pledge disputes. Two mortgagees each claim a pledge from different owners on the same property. The key is not just possession but the availability of a proper litigant—the mortgagor. The ruling changes depending on whether the alleged owners are present in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two mortgagees (A and B) each claim the same property was pledged to them by different owners (X and Y). The property is in A's hand.
2
Determine presence of mortgagors
Check if X and Y are present in court, absent, or one present and the other absent.
Presence of a proper litigant (khasm) is crucial.
3
Apply rule for both absent
If both mortgagors are absent, the possessor (A) wins. The outsider's evidence lacks a litigant.
4
Apply rule for both present
If both mortgagors are present, the outsider (B) wins. Each mortgagor can be a litigant.
Analogy to absolute ownership case.
5
Handle mixed presence and note Isa's objection
If only outsider's mortgagor present, possessor wins. If only possessor's mortgagor present, Isa argues the outsider wins. This is an intra-Hanafi difference.
Isa's view: possessor's mortgagor can be a litigant for the outsider.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني لو ادعيا الرهن من اثنين فأقام كل واحد البينة على الاتهان من آخر والرهن في يد أحدهما فلا يخلو إما أن يكون الراهنان غائبين أو كانا حاضرين أو أحدهما حاضر والآخر غائب، فإن كانا غائبين فذو اليد أولى ، وإن كان الخارج أسبق تاريخاً لأن بينة الخارج لا تسمع لأنها لم تقم على خصم لأن ذا اليد أثبتت بينته كونها رهناً في حق ما في يده والمرتهن لا ينتصب خصماً على المالك كالمودع فكان الشيء رهناً في يد ذي اليد كما يدعيه فإن كان الراهنان حاضرين فالخارج أولى لأن كل واحد من الراهنين ينتصب خصماً لصاحبه لأنه يدعي المدعي ويجعل إقامته البينة من المرتهنين وهما يحتاجان إلى إثبات ملك الراهنين ليصح رهنهما يمنزلة ما لو أقام الراهنان البينة على الملك المطلق والشيء في يد أحدهما كان الخارج أولى فكذا هذا. وإن كان راهن الخارج حاضراً وراهن ذي اليد غائباً فذو اليد أولى لأن المرتهن لا ينتصب خصماً لمن يدعي ملكاً في الرهن كالمودع فبينة الخارج قامت لا على خصم، وإن كان راهن ذي اليد حاضراً وراهن الخارج عائباً فكذلك طعن عيسى رحمه الله تعالى وقال: حضرة راهن ذي اليد تكفي للقضاء للخارج لأن راهن ذي اليد انتصب خصماً للخارج لأنه الملك لنفسه والرهن من ذي اليد والخارج مرتهن والمرتهن بمنزلة المودع والمودع ينتصب خصماً أنه ملكه ورهنه من يدعي
And as for the second type, if they each claim a pledge from two different men, and each one presents evidence of having taken a pledge from another, and the pledge is in the hand of one of them, then the situation is either that both mortgagors are absent, or both present, or one present and the other absent. If both are absent, the one in possession is preferred, even if the outsider has an earlier date, because the evidence of the outsider is not heard because it is not against a litigant, since the possessor's evidence establishes that it is a pledge with respect to what is in his hand, and the mortgagee cannot stand as a litigant against the owner like the depositary; so the property is a pledge in the hand of the possessor as he claims. If both mortgagors are present, the outsider is preferred, because each of the two mortgagors can be a litigant against the other, because he claims the plaintiff and makes his evidence from the mortgagees, and they need to prove the ownership of the mortgagors to validate their pledge, like the case where the two mortgagors present evidence of absolute ownership and the property is in the hand of one of them, the outsider is preferred. So also here. If the outsider's mortgagor is present and the possessor's mortgagor is absent, the possessor is preferred, because the mortgagee cannot stand as a litigant against one who claims ownership in the pledge, like the depositary, so the outsider's evidence is not against a litigant. If the possessor's mortgagor is present and the outsider's mortgagor is absent, similarly? Isa, may Allah have mercy on him, objected and said: The presence of the possessor's mortgagor is sufficient for judgment for the outsider, because the possessor's mortgagor stands as a litigant against the outsider, since he is the owner of himself and the pledge from the possessor, and the outsider is a mortgagee, and the mortgagee is like a depositary, and the depositary stands as a litigant that it is his property and his pledge from the one who claims.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage discusses the second category of competing claims: each claimant alleges a pledge from a different person (i.e., two different mortgagors claim ownership of the same property and it is currently in the possession of one of the mortgagees? Actually the scenario: two claimants (mortgagees) each claim that the property was pledged to them by different owners (mortgagors). The property is in the hand of one of the mortgagees. The ruling depends on the presence or absence of the alleged mortgagors. If both mortgagors are absent (i.e., not present in court), the mortgagee in possession is preferred. This is because his evidence is supported by his possession, and the other mortgagee's evidence is not heard since his case lacks a proper defendant (the absent mortgagor cannot be litigated against). The mortgagee in possession's evidence establishes his right to hold the property as pledge. If both mortgagors are present, then the outsider (the one not in possession) is preferred. Why? Because each mortgagor can be a litigant: the possessor's mortgagor can defend his property, and the outsider's mortgagor can support the outsider's claim. The outsider's evidence is now heard because it is against the possessor's mortgagor who is present. This is analogous to a case where both mortgagors present evidence of absolute ownership, and the property is in the hand of one; the outsider's claim is preferred because his evidence is against a present litigant. If only the outsider's mortgagor is present and the possessor's mortgagor is absent, the possessor remains preferred. If only the possessor's mortgagor is present and the outsider's mortgagor is absent, the text mentions an objection by Isa (likely a scholar) saying that the presence of the possessor's mortgagor is sufficient to allow judgment for the outsider because the mortgagor can stand as a litigant for the outsider. This indicates a nuance: the possessor's mortgagor can be a defendant for the outsider's claim, enabling the outsider's evidence to be heard. The passage ends with this objection, indicating a difference of opinion even within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
خصم (khasm): Litigant or opponent in a legal dispute; the presence of the mortgagor as a litigant is necessary for the case to proceed.
المودع (al-mudi'): Depositary; compared to the mortgagee in that both hold property but cannot represent the owner in litigation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a case where two mortgagees each claim a pledge from different owners on the same property, and both mortgagors are present in court, who is preferred?
A) The mortgagee in possession
B) The outsider (non-possessor)
C) Neither
D) The one with earlier date

Key Takeaway
The priority between two mortgagees claiming from different owners hinges on the presence of the mortgagors as litigants, not merely on possession or date.

Ruling on Two Claimants of the Same Pledge
Explains the ruling when two claimants each assert a pledge from the same debtor during his life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants each claim the same pledge from a living debtor, distinguishing the scenarios of possession and dating, and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas in the case of equal evidence without possession.

Core Idea
Possession gives preponderance; with dates, earlier wins; without either, istihsan gives each half the pledged item.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, Imam al-Marghinani addresses a dispute where two creditors each claim the same pledged item from the same debtor. The ruling depends on who holds the item and whether the pledges are dated. This lesson unpacks the three scenarios and the Hanafi preference for istihsan over strict qiyas when evidence is equal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios based on possession and dating
The passage presents three main situations: (1) the pledge is in the hand of one claimant, with no dates; (2) the pledges are dated; (3) there is no possession by either claimant and no dates. Recognize these as the branches of the ruling.
2
Apply the rule for possession preponderance
When the pledge is in one claimant's hand and no dates are given, that claimant is given preference because his possession (yad) strengthens his evidence. The hand indicates prior right, so his claim is accepted fully.
This is based on the maxim 'al-yad amara' — possession is a strong indicator.
3
Apply the rule for dating
If both claimants provide dates, the one with the earlier date wins. The later evidence is considered invalid because it contradicts the earlier established right.
The later claimant's bayyinah is rejected as it would nullify a prior valid contract.
4
Apply qiyas and istihsan for the no-possession, no-date case
When neither has possession and there are no dates, strict qiyas would reject both claims because evidence is equal and there is no preponderance. However, Hanafi istihsan gives each claimant half of the pledged item as security for half his debt, because both pledges are valid contracts and the item becomes shared collateral.
This is the key methodological point: istihsan overrides qiyas for equity.
5
Understand the reasoning behind istihsan
The istihsan ruling is based on the principle that both pledges are established by equally strong evidence, so the pledged item is detained for both rights. Rejecting both would cause injustice to the creditors, as their contracts would be nullified without cause.
Istihsan here is a departure from strict analogy based on necessity (darurah) and custom (urf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو طلبا لا تقبل لأنهما يشهدان لأنفسهما برهن ويسعيان في نقض عقد تم بينهما وليس لهما النقض. ادعيا على رجل أن كل واحد له الرهن فهي على قسمين، أما إذا كان الرهن في يد أحدهما أو في أيديهما أو في يد الراهن والدعوى منهما حال حياة الراهن أو بعد وفاته وقد أرخا ذلك كله أو لم يؤرخا فإن كان الرهن في يد أحدهما ولم يؤرخا فهو أولى لأنه قد ترجحت بينة ذي اليد باليد لأن يده تدل على أنه سبق ارتهانه ولأن يده صحيحة من حيث الظاهر فلا يجوز نقضها إلا أن يعلم بطلانها كما لو ادعيا الشراء من واحد والمبيع في يد أحدهما، فإن أرخا يقضى لأسبقهما تاريخاً لأن البينة التي آخرهما تاريخاً غير مقبولة لأنها قامت فاسد وكان الذي هو أسبق انفرد بإقامة البينة، وإن لم يؤرخا لا يقضى لهما قياساً وفي الاستحسان لكل واحد نصفه بنصف حقه لأن رهن كل واحد منهما ثبت بينتهما معاً فصح الرهن فصار العبد محبوساً بحق كل واحد منهما على الكمال. هذا كله في حال حياة الراهن.
And if both demand (the pledge), it is not accepted because they testify for themselves regarding a pledge and strive to nullify a contract concluded between them, and they have no right to nullify. If two men claim against a man that each has the pledge, then it is of two divisions: when the pledge is in the hand of one of them, or in their hands, or in the hand of the pledgor, and the claim is from them during the life of the pledgor or after his death, and they have either dated it all or not dated. If the pledge is in the hand of one of them and they have not dated, then he is more entitled because the evidence of the possessor is preponderated by possession, for his hand indicates that his pledge was prior, and because his possession is valid outwardly, so it is not permissible to nullify it unless its invalidity is known, as if two men claim purchase of one thing and the sold item is in the hand of one. If they have dated, judgment is given to the one with earlier date because the evidence of the one with later date is unacceptable because it was established invalid, and the one earlier was alone in presenting evidence. And if they have not dated, no judgment is given to them by qiyas, but by istihsan each gets half for half his right because the pledge of each of them is established by their evidence together, so the pledge is valid, so the slave becomes detained by the full right of each. All this is during the life of the pledgor.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the book of Rahn. The author, Imam al-Marghinani, presents the case where two claimants each claim a pledge from the same debtor. The ruling is detailed based on whether the pledge is in the possession of one claimant, both, or the debtor, and whether the claims are dated or not. The key principle is that during the debtor's lifetime, possession (al-yad) gives priority to the possessor's claim if no dates are given, because the hand is prima facie evidence of rightful possession. If dates are given, the earlier date prevails. If no dates and no possession, the strict analogy (qiyas) would dismiss both claims, but the author prefers istihsan (juristic preference), ruling that each claimant gets half of the pledged item as security for half his debt. This is because both claims are established equally, so the item becomes shared collateral. The istihsan here is based on the need to avoid injustice and facilitate transactions. The commentary emphasizes that this is the relied-upon position of the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
يد (yad): Possession or hand, used as prima facie evidence of ownership or right.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, usually testimony of two witnesses or other proof.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to derive a ruling for a new case based on an original case with a common effective cause.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that abandons strict analogy in favor of a more equitable outcome.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two claimants each claim a pledged item from the same debtor. The item is in the hand of one claimant, and neither claim is dated. According to the Hanafi ruling, what happens?
A) The claim is rejected for both.
B) The claimant in possession gets the entire pledge for his full debt.
C) The claimant in possession is given preference and gets the pledge.
D) Each gets half of the pledge as security for half his debt.

Key Takeaway
When evidence is balanced and no possession or dates, Hanafi istihsan gives each claimant half the pledged item, overriding the strict qiyas of dismissing both.

Ruling on Two Claimants to a Pledge After Debtor's Death
Explains the dispute between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the fate of the pledge after the debtor's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the validity of a pledge of an undivided share after the debtor's death and distinguish it from the position of Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
After death, the pledge's purpose shifts from possession to value; thus, a pledge of an undivided share becomes valid according to Muhammad.

This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether two creditors can each claim half of a pledged item after the debtor dies. The key question is whether the prohibition of pledging undivided shares (rahn musha') applies after death, when physical possession is impossible. The disagreement between Imam Muhammad (with Abu Hanifa) and Imam Abu Yusuf has practical implications for how creditors recover their debts.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the scenario: after the debtor's death, two separate claimants each present evidence of a valid pledge from the debtor. The question is whether each can enforce a half‑share in the same pledged item.
The item cannot be physically divided during life due to the nature of rahn musha'.
2
Identify the two positions
Muhammad (and Abu Hanifa) hold that each gets half of the item as a pledge for half his debt, and the item is sold. Abu Yusuf argues that this is invalid; the claimants get nothing from the item.
The opposing school here is an intra-Hanafi opponent, not a different madhhab.
3
Follow Abu Yusuf's proof
He uses qiyas: just as a pledge of an undivided share is invalid during life because it requires exclusive possession, the same applies after death. Therefore, the item should go to the general creditors.
His ruling: the item is part of the estate and all creditors share equally.
4
Analyze Muhammad's response
Muhammad distinguishes the post‑death context: the objective of a pledge after death is not physical possession (since the debtor is dead) but the legal right to sell and recover the debt. Thus, the prohibition of rahn musha' does not apply.
The item is sold, and each claimant gets half of the proceeds up to his debt; surplus goes to other creditors.
5
Determine the thamarah and tarjih
The practical difference: under Muhammad, each claimant recovers half his debt from the specific item; under Abu Yusuf, they must share with all creditors. The mufta bihi is Muhammad's view as it is more equitable and aligns with the purpose of securing debts.
This is the majority position within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما بعد وفاته لو أقام كل واحد البينة على ارتهانه منه يقضى لكل واحد بنصفه رهناً بنصف حقه يباع فيه عندهما وما بقي للغرماء. أحدهما على رهن نأخذ وبه. وقال أبو يوسف: لا يقضى لهما بشيء وهو قول الغرماء بالحصص قياساً لأن القضاء بالرهن منهما قضاء برهن مشاع وأنه باطل كما في حالة الحياة لهما أن القصد مطلوب بحكمه لا بعينه لأنه شرع ليكون وسيلة وذريعة إلى حكمه وحكمه الرهن بعد الموت في حق هذا الحكم بخلاف حال الحياة لأن ثمة المقصود من الرهن هو ملك اليد والحبس ولا يملك اثنان اليد والحبس في المشاع دائماً فلا يمكن القضاء بالرهن.
As for after his death, if each presents evidence of his pledge from him, judgment is given to each for half of it as a pledge for half his right, and it is sold for both according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), and what remains is for the creditors. One of them? on a pledge we take and with it. And Abu Yusuf said: No judgment is given to them for anything, and this is the statement of the creditors for shares by qiyas, because judgment of the pledge from both is judgment of a pledge of an undivided share, and that is invalid as in the case of life; that the objective is sought by its ruling not by its essence because it was legislated to be a means and a conduit to its ruling, and its ruling is pledge after death regarding this ruling unlike the case of life because there the objective of the pledge is ownership of hand and detention, and two cannot own hand and detention in an undivided share permanently, so judgment of the pledge is not possible.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad (and by extension Abu Hanifa, as 'عندهما' indicates) and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the ruling when two claimants each prove a pledge from the same debtor after his death. According to Muhammad, each gets half of the pledged item as security for half his debt, and the item is sold to satisfy both, with any surplus going to other creditors. Abu Yusuf, however, argues that this is invalid because it would involve a pledge of an undivided share (رهن مشاع), which is impermissible. He analogizes it to the situation during the debtor's life, where such a pledge would be invalid. Muhammad responds by distinguishing the post-death context: after death, the objective of the pledge is not physical detention but rather securing the debt through the item's value. Therefore, the prohibition of pledging an undivided share does not apply, as the item is sold and the proceeds distributed. This is a critical refinement within the Hanafi school, and the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Muhammad and Abu Hanifa, allowing each creditor to have half as security.

Key Terms
رهن مشاع (rahn musha'): A pledge of an undivided share in a jointly owned property.
عندهما (indahuma): According to the two (usually Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After the death of the debtor, two claimants prove pledges. According to the Hanafi school, what is the outcome?
A) The pledges are invalid because they involve undivided shares.
B) Each claimant gets half of the pledged item as security.
C) The item is returned to the debtor's estate proportionally.
D) The claimants must rely on other creditors.

Key Takeaway
After the debtor's death, the prohibition of pledging undivided shares is lifted because the objective is the value, not possession; thus each claimant can enforce a half‑share in the pledged item.

Priority Among Multiple Pledge Claimants Based on Debtor Presence
Ruling on two pledge claimants from two debtors with varying debtor presence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that evidence must be against a present opponent (khasm) to determine priority between two pledge claimants.

Core Idea
The pledgee cannot act as an opponent for the debtor; evidence from an outsider fails without a present debtor.

This lesson covers the first scenario in the passage: two claimants each claim a pledge from two different debtors, and the pledged item is in the hand of one claimant. The key principle is that the pledgee (مرتهن) is like a depositary (مودع) and cannot represent the debtor as a legal opponent. Therefore, the priority depends on whether the debtor of each claimant is present or absent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
Two claimants (A and B) each claim a pledge from two different debtors (RA and RB). The pledged item is in the hand of one claimant, say A. Determine who is the 'possessor' (ذو اليد) and who is the 'outsider' (خارج).
The possessor is the claimant holding the item.
2
Check the presence of the debtors
Determine whether both debtors are present or absent, or only one is present. The ruling changes accordingly.
Presence means the debtor is in court or can be summoned.
3
Apply the opponent rule
Evidence must be presented against a present opponent (خصم حاضر). The pledgee cannot act as opponent for his debtor because he is like a depositary (مودع). Thus, if a debtor is absent, evidence against him is not heard.
This is based on the analogy (qiyas) to the depositary.
4
Determine priority for each case
Case 1: Both debtors absent → possessor wins (outsider's evidence not against an opponent). Case 2: Both debtors present → outsider wins (each debtor opposes the other). Case 3: Only possessor's debtor present → possessor wins (outsider's evidence fails because his debtor is absent). Case 4: Only outsider's debtor present → possessor wins (same reason plus the outsider's debt claim also lacks opponent).
The text refutes 'Isa's objection that the present debtor of the possessor could serve as opponent for the outsider.
5
Analyze the objection and response
Scholar 'Isa argued that the debtor of the possessor could be an opponent for the outsider. The Hanafi response: the outsider also claims a debt from his absent debtor, and the pledge is invalid without the debt; evidence for the debt cannot be heard without the debtor present, so the pledge claim fails.
Thus the possessor's priority is upheld in all cases except when both debtors are present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني لو ادعيا الرهن من اثنين فأقام كل واحد البينة على الارتهان من آخر والرهن في يد أحدهما فلا يخلو إما أن يكون الراهنان غائبين أو كانا حاضرين أو أحدهما حاضر والآخر غائب، فإن كانا غائبين فذو اليد أولى، وإن كان الخارج أسبق تاريخاً لأن بينة الخارج لا تسمع لأنها لم تقم على خصم لأن ذا اليد أثبتت بينته كونها رهناً في حق ما في يده والمرتهن لا ينتصب خصماً على المالك كالمودع فكان الشيء رهناً في يد ذي اليد كما يدعيه فإن كان الراهنان حاضرين فالخارج أولى لأن كل واحد من الراهنين ينتصب خصماً لصاحبه لأنه يدعي المدعي ويجعل إقامته البينة من المرتهنين وهما يحتاجان إلى إثبات ملك الراهنين ليصح رهنهما بمنزلة ما لو أقام الراهنان البينة على الملك المطلق والشيء في يد أحدهما كان الخارج أولى فكذا هذا. وإن كان راهن الخارج حاضراً وراهن ذي اليد غائباً فذو اليد أولى لأن المرتهن لا ينتصب خصماً لمن يدعي ملكاً في الرهن كالمودع فبينة الخارج قامت لا على خصم، وإن كان راهن ذي اليد حاضراً وراهن الخارج غائباً فكذلك طعن عيسى رحمه الله تعالى وقال: حضرة راهن ذي اليد تكفي للقضاء للخارج لأن راهن ذي اليد انتصب خصماً للخارج لأنه الملك لنفسه والرهن من ذي اليد والخارج مرتهن والمرتهن بمنزلة المودع والمودع ينتصب خصماً لمن يدعي أنه ملكه ورهنه من يدعي له. والجواب عنه أن المرتهن كما يثبت الملك لراهنه يدعي ديناً وهو غائب وليس عنه خصم حاضر فلا تقبل بينته على إثبات الدين فلا تقبل على إثبات الرهن أيضاً لأن الرهن لا يصح بدون الدين بخلاف المودع لأنه لم يدع على مودعه شيئاً بل يدعي الملك له فينتصب في إثبات الملك له. ولو ادعى واحد على رجلين الرهن وأقام البينة على أحدهما أنه رهنه المتاع ويجحدان الرهن يستخلف من لم يقم عليه البينة فإن حلف رد الرهن عليهما لأنه لم يثبت الرهن في حقه فلا يقضي به في نصيب الآخر لأنه لا يكون قبضاً بالرهن في نصف مشاع وذلك لا يجوز، فإن نكل ثبت عليهما على الناكل بالنكول وعلى الآخر بالبينة وإن كان المرتهن اثنين والراهن واحد فأقام أحدهما البينة أني ارتهنت وصاحبي بمائة وأنكر الراهن والمرتهن الآخر الرهن يرد على الراهن عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد يقضى به رهناً ويجعل في يد المرتهن الذي أقام البينة وعلى يد عدل، فإن قضى الراهن المرتهن المقيم البينة فله أخذ الرهن، فإن هلك الرهن ذهب نصيبه لا نصيب الجاحد ولا رواية عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله فيه لمحمد أنه لا يمكن المدعي إثبات الرهن على الراهن إلا بعد إثباته على صاحبه لأن الرهن من اثنين لا يصح إلا بقبولهما جميعاً فكان الرهن من صاحبه سبباً لثبوت الرهن في حقه، ومن أنكر سبب ثبوت حق إنسان خصماً له فقامت البينة على خصمه كما لو ادعى عيناً في يد إنسان أنه اشتراها من فلان الغائب تقبل بينته على ذلك ومتى ثبت الرهن منهما يوضع في نوبة الجاحد على يد عدل لأن الرهن في حق الجاحد غير ثابت في حق المدعي والراهن ما رضي بحفظ المدعي وحده. ولأبي يوسف رحمه الله أن ما يدعيه على صاحبه ليس سبباً لثبوت حقه بل هو شرط لثبوت حقه لا قول صاحبه فلا يمكنه إثبات قول صاحبه وهو جاحد كما لو ادعيا رهناً من اثنين وهو في يد أحدهما وراهن ذي اليد حاضر لا تقبل بينة الخارج على إثبات الرهن على الغائب كما بينا فكذا هذا والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم بالصواب وإليه المرجع والمآب.
As for the second division: if two men claim the pledge from two (different men), and each presents evidence of pledging from another, and the pledge is in the hand of one of the two claimants, then it is not free from: either both debtors are absent, or both present, or one present and the other absent. If both are absent, then the possessor is more entitled, even if the outsider's evidence is earlier in date, because the outsider's evidence is not heard as it was not established against a opponent, since the possessor's evidence establishes it as a pledge regarding what is in his hand, and the pledgee does not stand as opponent against the owner like the depositary, so the thing is a pledge in the hand of the possessor as he claims. If both debtors are present, then the outsider is more entitled because each of the debtors stands as opponent for the other, since he claims the claimant, and he makes the presentation of evidence from the two pledgees, and both need to prove the ownership of the debtors for their pledge to be valid, like the case where the two debtors present evidence of absolute ownership and the thing is in the hand of one, then the outsider is more entitled, so here too. And if the debtor of the outsider is present and the debtor of the possessor is absent, then the possessor is more entitled because the pledgee does not stand as opponent for one who claims ownership in the pledge, like the depositary, so the outsider's evidence is not against an opponent. And if the debtor of the possessor is present and the debtor of the outsider is absent, then likewise. Isa (may Allah have mercy on him) objected saying: The presence of the debtor of the possessor is sufficient for judgment for the outsider because the debtor of the possessor stands as opponent for the outsider, as he is the owner for himself and the pledge from the possessor, and the outsider is a pledgee, and the pledgee is like the depositary, and the depositary stands as opponent for one who claims that it is his property and that he pledged it to the one who claims for him. The answer to him: The pledgee, as he establishes ownership for his debtor, also claims a debt, and he (the debtor) is absent, and there is no present opponent for him, so his evidence to establish the debt is not accepted, so also his evidence to establish the pledge is not accepted, because the pledge is not valid without the debt, unlike the depositary because he does not claim anything against his depositor but rather claims ownership for him, so he stands as opponent in establishing ownership for him. And if one man claims a pledge from two men and presents evidence against one that he pledged the item to him, and they both deny the pledge, then the one against whom evidence was not presented is asked to take an oath; if he swears, the pledge is returned to them both because the pledge is not established against him, so it is not judged for the other's share because that would be a pledge in half undivided, and that is not permissible. If he refuses to swear, it is established against both: against the one who refused by his refusal, and against the other by the evidence. And if the pledgees are two and the debtor is one, and one of them presents evidence that 'I and my companion pledged on a hundred,' and the debtor and the other pledgee deny the pledge, it is returned to the debtor according to Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad, it is judged as a pledge and placed in the hand of the pledgee who presented evidence and in the hand of a trustworthy person. Then if the debtor pays the presenting pledgee, he may take the pledge; if the pledge is destroyed, his share is lost, not the share of the denier. There is no narration from Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) from Muhammad that the claimant cannot establish the pledge against the debtor except after establishing it against his companion, because a pledge from two is not valid except by acceptance of both, so the pledge from his companion is a cause for establishing the pledge against him, and the one who denies the cause of establishing a right is an opponent for him, so evidence is established against his opponent, as if one claims a chattel in the hand of a person that he bought it from so-and-so who is absent, his evidence is accepted for that. And once the pledge from both is established, it is placed in the share of the denier in the hand of a trustworthy person because the pledge regarding the denier is not established in the claim of the claimant, and the debtor did not consent to the claimant's custody alone. For Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him): what he claims against his companion is not a cause for establishing his right but a condition, not the statement of his companion, so he cannot establish the statement of his companion while he denies, as in the case of two claiming a pledge from two while it is in the hand of one and the debtor of the possessor is present, the evidence of the outsider is not accepted to establish the pledge against the absent one, as we explained. And Allah Most High knows best the truth, and to Him is the return and final abode.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This lengthy passage covers two distinct but related scenarios. The first is the 'second division' (القسم الثاني) where two claimants each claim a pledge from two different debtors, and the pledged item is in the possession of one of the claimants. The ruling depends on whether the debtors are present or absent. If both debtors are absent, the possessor wins because the other claimant cannot establish his case against an absent debtor (the pledgee cannot act as opponent). If both debtors are present, the outsider wins because each debtor can oppose the other. If only the debtor of the possessor is present, the possessor wins; if only the debtor of the outsider is present, there is a known objection by 'Isa (a Hanafi scholar) who argued that the presence of the debtor of the possessor suffices for the outsider, but the text refutes this by noting that the pledgee also claims a debt, and the debtor of the outsider is absent, so evidence for the debt cannot be heard, and the pledge depends on the debt. The second scenario involves one claimant against two joint debtors (or two pledgees against one debtor). If one claimant has evidence against one debtor but not the other, the ruling involves oaths; if the unproven debtor swears, the claim fails; if he refuses, it succeeds. Then the case of two pledgees (مرتهنان) against one debtor (راهن): one presents evidence that both pledged together. According to Abu Yusuf, the claim is rejected; according to Muhammad, it is accepted and the item is placed in the hand of the proving pledgee and a trustee. The passage ends with the reasoning of both imams. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Muhammad's view, as it facilitates the transaction.

Key Terms
خصم (khasm): Opponent in litigation; the person against whom evidence is presented.
مرتهن (murtahin): Pledgee; the person who receives the pledge as security for a debt.
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): Possessor; the person who currently holds the pledged item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two claimants each claim a pledge from two different debtors. The pledged item is in the hand of one claimant. Both debtors are present. According to the Hanafi school, who is more entitled?
A) The possessor, because he has possession.
B) The outsider, because each debtor can act as opponent for the other.
C) The court holds the item until the debtors decide.
D) Neither, because the debtors are present and can claim their own property.

Key Takeaway
In disputes over pledges, the presence of the debtor as an opponent is crucial; the pledgee cannot represent an absent debtor.

Dispute Over Joint Pledge: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on enforcement of a joint pledge when only one pledgee proves it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding a pledge claimed by two pledgees from one debtor, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
When two pledgees claim a joint pledge, Muhammad enforces it; Abu Yusuf rejects it unless both agree.

This lesson addresses the second scenario in the passage: two pledgees (مرتهنان) claim that they jointly received a pledge from a single debtor (راهن). One of the pledgees presents evidence, but the other pledgee denies the pledge. The Hanafi school has a known dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the validity of such a claim. The passage also includes a brief note about the role of the debtor's consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and claim
There is one debtor (راهن) and two pledgees (مرتهنان). One pledgee presents evidence that he and his companion jointly pledged the item for a debt of 100. The debtor and the other pledgee both deny the pledge.
The evidence is only from one pledgee; the other denies.
2
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that the claim is rejected. The pledge is returned to the debtor because the claimant cannot prove his companion's consent, and a joint pledge requires acceptance by both pledgees.
Abu Yusuf sees the companion's denial as fatal.
3
State Muhammad's position
Muhammad holds that the pledge is enforced. It is placed in the hand of the proving pledgee and a trustworthy person (عدل). If the debtor pays the proving pledgee, he may take the pledge. If the pledge perishes, only the proving pledgee's share is lost.
Muhammad views the claim against the companion as a condition, not a cause.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Muhammad analogizes to a case where one claims a chattel from a possessor, saying he bought it from an absent person. The evidence is accepted because the absent person's denial is not a bar. Similarly, the companion's denial does not prevent establishing the pledge against the debtor. Abu Yusuf argues that the companion's statement is necessary; without it, the pledge cannot be proven.
The passage notes no narration from Abu Hanifa on this point.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Muhammad's view, as it facilitates transactions and is from the Zahir al-Riwayah. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq sides with Muhammad.
Practically, this means the court can enforce a joint pledge even if one pledgee later denies.

Source Text

الملك له. ولو ادعى واحد على رجلين الرهن وأقام البينة على أحدهما أنه رهنه المتاع ويجحدان الرهن يستخلف من لم يقم عليه البينة فإن حلف رد الرهن عليهما لأنه لم يثبت الرهن في حقه فلا يقضي به في نصيب الآخر لأنه لا يكون قبضاً بالرهن في نصف مشاع وذلك لا يجوز، فإن نكل ثبت عليهما على الناكل بالنكول وعلى الآخر بالبينة وإن كان المرتهن اثنين والراهن واحد فأقام أحدهما البينة أني ارتهنت وصاحبي بمائة وأنكر الراهن لثبوت الرهن في حقه، ومن أنكر سبب ثبوت حق إنسان خصماً له فقامت البينة على خصمه كما لو ادعى عيناً في يد إنسان أنه اشتراها من فلان الغائب تقبل بينته على ذلك ومتى ثبت الرهن منهما يوضع في نوبة الجاحد على صاحبه وهو جاحد كما لو ادعيا رهناً من اثنين وهو في يد أحدهما وراهن ذي اليد حاضر لا تقبل بينة الخارج على إثبات الرهن على الغائب كما بينا فكذا هذا والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم بالصواب وإليه المرجع والمآب.
As for the second division: if two men claim the pledge from two (different men), and each presents evidence of pledging from another, and the pledge is in the hand of one of the two claimants, then it is not free from: either both debtors are absent, or both present, or one present and the other absent. If both are absent, then the possessor is more entitled, even if the outsider's evidence is earlier in date, because the outsider's evidence is not heard as it was not established against a opponent, since the possessor's evidence establishes it as a pledge regarding what is in his hand, and the pledgee does not stand as opponent against the owner like the depositary, so the thing is a pledge in the hand of the possessor as he claims. If both debtors are present, then the outsider is more entitled because each of the debtors stands as opponent for the other, since he claims the claimant, and he makes the presentation of evidence from the two pledgees, and both need to prove the ownership of the debtors for their pledge to be valid, like the case where the two debtors present evidence of absolute ownership and the thing is in the hand of one, then the outsider is more entitled, so here too. And if the debtor of the outsider is present and the debtor of the possessor is absent, then the possessor is more entitled because the pledgee does not stand as opponent for one who claims ownership in the pledge, like the depositary, so the outsider's evidence is not against an opponent. And if the debtor of the possessor is present and the debtor of the outsider is absent, then likewise. Isa (may Allah have mercy on him) objected saying: The presence of the debtor of the possessor is sufficient for judgment for the outsider because the debtor of the possessor stands as opponent for the outsider, as he is the owner for himself and the pledge from the possessor, and the outsider is a pledgee, and the pledgee is like the depositary, and the depositary stands as opponent for one who claims that it is his property and that he pledged it to the one who claims for him. If he refuses to swear, it is established against both: against the one who refused by his refusal, and against the other by the evidence. According to Muhammad, it is judged as a pledge and placed in the hand of the pledgee who presented evidence and in the hand of a trustworthy person. Then if the debtor pays the presenting pledgee, he may take the pledge; if the pledge is destroyed, his share is lost, not the share of the denier. And once the pledge from both is established, it is placed in the share of the denier in the hand of a trustworthy person because the pledge regarding the denier is not established in the claim of the claimant, and the debtor did not consent to the claimant's custody alone. And Allah Most High knows best the truth, and to Him is the return and final abode.

Commentary
This lengthy passage covers two distinct but related scenarios. The first is the 'second division' (القسم الثاني) where two claimants each claim a pledge from two different debtors, and the pledged item is in the possession of one of the claimants. The ruling depends on whether the debtors are present or absent. If both debtors are absent, the possessor wins because the other claimant cannot establish his case against an absent debtor (the pledgee cannot act as opponent). If both debtors are present, the outsider wins because each debtor can oppose the other. If only the debtor of the possessor is present, the possessor wins; if only the debtor of the outsider is present, there is a known objection by 'Isa (a Hanafi scholar) who argued that the presence of the debtor of the possessor suffices for the outsider, but the text refutes this by noting that the pledgee also claims a debt, and the debtor of the outsider is absent, so evidence for the debt cannot be heard, and the pledge depends on the debt. The second scenario involves one claimant against two joint debtors (or two pledgees against one debtor). If one claimant has evidence against one debtor but not the other, the ruling involves oaths; if the unproven debtor swears, the claim fails; if he refuses, it succeeds. Then the case of two pledgees (مرتهنان) against one debtor (راهن): one presents evidence that both pledged together. According to Abu Yusuf, the claim is rejected; according to Muhammad, it is accepted and the item is placed in the hand of the proving pledgee and a trustee. The passage ends with the reasoning of both imams. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Muhammad's view, as it facilitates the transaction.

Key Terms
مرتهن (murtahin): Pledgee; the person who receives the pledge as security for a debt.
جاحد (jahid): Denier; the party who denies the claim (here, the second pledgee who denies the joint pledge).
عدل (adl): Trustworthy person; a court-appointed custodian for property in dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding a joint pledge claimed by two pledgees, which position is the mufta bihi (relied upon for fatwa)?
A) Abu Yusuf's position
B) Muhammad's position
C) Neither, because there is no narration from Abu Hanifa
D) Both are equally valid

Key Takeaway
Muhammad's view that a joint pledge is enforceable on the evidence of one pledgee, despite the other's denial, is the operative ruling in the Hanafi school.

Pledging an Undivided Share: Ruling and Liability
Defines the ruling on pledging undivided shares.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pledging an undivided share and explain the consequence of possession (daman).

Core Idea
Pledging an undivided share is invalid; possession triggers liability.

This lesson opens a new chapter on the permissibility of pledge (rahn). The author first addresses the undivided share (musha')—a share in joint property that is not physically partitioned. The Hanafi position is clear: such a pledge is not valid. But because the contract is fasid (voidable) rather than batil (void), taking possession creates a liability for the pledgee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is pledging an undivided share (رهن المشاع). The ruling: لا يجوز (not permissible), meaning the contract is invalid (fasid).
Not haram but legally ineffective.
2
Understand the term 'مشاع'
Musha' refers to an undivided share in jointly owned property. The Hanafis treat it as incapable of exclusive possession, which is required for a valid pledge.
Contrast with divided (مقسوم) property.
3
Learn the consequence of possession
If the pledgee takes possession despite the invalidity, he becomes liable (ضمان) for the item. He must compensate the owner if it is lost.
This is typical for fasid contracts in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The apparent meaning of the text makes no distinction between divisible and indivisible property. Some later scholars like صاحب العناية affirm the ruling for both cases, confirming the general rule.
No practical difference in the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يجوز ارتهانه والارتهان به وما لا يجوز لما ذكر مقدمات مسائل الرهن ذكر في هذا الباب تفصيل ما يجوز ارتهانه والارتهان به وما لا يجوز إذ التفصيل إنما يكون بعد الإجمال. قال رحمه الله : ولا يجوز رهن المشاع) يعني لا يصح رهن المشاع فظاهره أنه لا فرق بين ما يحتمل القسمة وما لا يحتمل القسمة. قال صاحب العناية : رهن المشاع قابل القسمة وغيره فاسد يتعلق به الضمان إذا قبض، وقيل
Chapter on what is permissible to pledge and take as pledge and what is not permissible. When he mentioned the preliminaries of the issues of pledge, he mentioned in this chapter the detail of what is permissible to pledge and take as pledge and what is not permissible, for detail only comes after summary. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And it is not permissible to pledge an undivided share (musha'), meaning a pledge of an undivided share is not valid. Its apparent meaning is that there is no difference between what is divisible and what is not divisible. The author of al-'Inayah said: A pledge of an undivided share, whether divisible or not, is invalid but liability attaches if possession is taken. And it is said...
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This is the beginning of a new chapter (باب) in the book of Rahn. The author, Imam al-Marghinani, introduces the chapter by stating that after discussing the preliminary issues of pledge, he will now detail what can and cannot be pledged. The first ruling is that pledging an undivided share (مشاع) is not permissible (لا يجوز). The commentator (the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) explains that this means the pledge is invalid (فساد) according to the apparent meaning of the text, without distinction between property that is divisible or not. He then quotes from صاحب العناية (likely a reference to al-'Inayah) that the pledge of an undivided share is fasid (voidable), and if the pledgee takes possession, he incurs liability (ضمان) for the item, meaning he must compensate if it is lost. This is the standard Hanafi position: the contract of rahn is invalid for مشاع, but since it is not batil (void), possession creates some obligations. The phrase 'وقيل' indicates an alternative opinion, which is likely from other Hanafi authorities. This passage serves as a foundation for the chapter.

Key Terms
مشاع (musha'): An undivided share in joint property; a portion that is not partitioned.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on pledging an undivided share (مشاع) in the Hanafi school?
A) It is valid if the property is divisible.
B) It is valid if both parties agree.
C) It is not permissible (invalid), and if possession is taken, the pledgee becomes liable.
D) It is permissible but makruh.

Key Takeaway
Pledging an undivided share (musha') is invalid (fasid) in Hanafi law; possession creates liability, not validity.

Partial Recovery of Pledged Property: Specific Location vs Undivided Share
Ruling on when a pledged property is partially recovered by rightful owner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between a specific location and an undivided share to determine the validity of a pledge after partial recovery.

Core Idea
Partial recovery voids the pledge only if the remaining part cannot be pledged independently.

What happens to a pledge when it is discovered that part of the property does not belong to the pledgor? In Hanafi law, the answer depends not on the fact of recovery itself, but on the nature of the recovered portion. This lesson examines the key distinction between a specific location and an undivided share, and how the qiyas principle of 'valid in inception implies validity in continuation' governs the outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios of partial recovery
The passage presents two cases: recovery of a specific location (موضع معين) and recovery of an undivided share (جزء شائع) or something similar. Note each and their legal consequences.
2
Apply the initial pledgeability test
Ask: Could the remaining part have been validly pledged at the start of the contract? If yes, the pledge continues; if no, the pledge is void.
This is the core illa (effective cause) in the qiyas analogy.
3
Analyze the qiyas reasoning
The author uses a clear qiyas jaliy: the ability to pledge initially (جواز ابتداء الرهن) is the illa for validity in continuation. Verify the analogy from asl (initial pledge) to far' (remaining part after recovery).
4
Distinguish the two categories
A specific location (e.g., a room) is divisible and can be pledged separately; an undivided share cannot be physically isolated, so the initial pledge would have been invalid, making the entire contract void.
The example of dates (تمر) illustrates something that is essentially an undivided share in bulk.
5
Derive the practical ruling
Formulate the ruling: Pledge remains valid on the rest if the recovered part is a specific location; the entire pledge becomes void if the recovered part is an undivided share or equivalent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الرهن باب ما يجوز ارتهانه والارتهان به وما لا يجوز قليل أو كثير هو فيها أو منها لا يدخل المتاع وهذه الأشياء تدخل وكذا تدخل هذه الأشياء في رهن الدار والقرية لما ذكرنا ولو استحق بعضه إن كان الباقي يجوز ابتداء الرهن عليه وأخذه جاز وذلك بأن يكون المستحق موضعاً معيناً لأن رهنه ابتداء يجوز فكذا بقاء، وإن كان الباقي لا يجوز ابتداء الرهن عليه بأن استحق جزءاً شائعاً أو ما هو في معنى الشائع كالتمر ونحوه بطل لأنه تبين بالاستحقاق أن الرهن وقع باطلاً
Book of Pledge: Chapter on what is valid to pledge and what is valid to pledge upon and what is not. A little or a lot that is in it or from it, the goods do not enter, and these things enter. Likewise, these things enter in the pledge of a house and a village due to what we mentioned. And if part of it is recovered, if the remainder is valid for an initial pledge upon it, it is permissible, and that is when the recovered part is a specific location, because an initial pledge upon it is valid, so likewise it remains. And if the remainder is not valid for an initial pledge upon it, such as when an undivided share is recovered, or what is like an undivided share like dates and the like, it becomes void because it becomes clear through recovery that the pledge was void.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the validity of a pledge when part of the pledged property is found to be owned by someone else (istihqaq). The author first establishes that in the pledge of a house or village, immovable fixtures and attached items (like trees, buildings) are included, but movable goods (متاع) are not. Then, if a portion of the property is legally claimed by another, the ruling depends on the nature of that portion. If the recovered part is a specific, identifiable location (موضع معين), the pledge remains valid on the remainder because that remainder could have been pledged initially—validity in inception implies validity in continuation (قياس: جواز ابتداء الرهن يستلزم جواز البقاء). However, if the recovered part is an undivided share (جزء شائع) or something similar (like dates mixed in bulk), the entire pledge becomes void (باطل). The reason is that the original pledge was inherently invalid from the start because it included property not owned by the pledgor—an undivided share makes the contract impossible to execute partially. This is a clear application of the Hanafi principle that a contract must be valid in its foundation; if part is void and inseparable, the whole is void.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqāq): Legal claim by a third party that part of the pledged property rightfully belongs to them, invalidating the pledgor's ownership over that portion.
جزء شائع (juzʾ shāʾiʿ): An undivided share in a property (e.g., half of a house) that cannot be physically separated.
موضع معين (mawḍiʿ muʿayyan): A specific, physically identifiable location within a property (e.g., a room, a garage) that can be separated from the rest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a house is pledged, and it is later discovered that a specific room belongs to someone else, what happens to the pledge of the rest of the house?
A) The entire pledge becomes void because part is invalid
B) The pledge remains valid on the rest because the room is a specific location that could have been pledged separately
C) The pledge is valid only if the creditor agrees
D) The pledge is suspended until the dispute is resolved

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, partial recovery of pledged property voids the pledge only if the remaining part could not have been pledged initially; a specific location allows continuation, an undivided share does not.
