بعينه في إثبات هذا المعنى العام فتأمل . قال صاحب العناية : ولقائل أن يقول ما يرجع إلى المحل فالابتداء والبقاء فيه سواء فما بال هذا ذلك الأصل؟ وقال : ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأنه كذلك فيما يكون المحل باقياً وههنا يتبدل المحل حكماً بتبدل الوصف فكذلك تخلف عن ذلك الأصل ا هـ . . أقول : قوله : «ثم تخلل وهي تساوي عشرة» يشير إلى أن المعتبر فيه في الزيادة والنقصان القيمة وليس كذلك بل المعتبر القدر لأن العصير والخل من المقدرات لأنه إما مكيل أو موزون وفيها نقصان القيمة لا يوجب سقوط شيء من ذلك الدين كما مر في انكسار القلب، وإنما يوجب الخيار على ما ذكرنا لأن الغاية فيه مجرد الوصف وفوات كل شيء من الوصف في المكيل والموزون لا يوجب سقوط شيء من الدين بإجماع بين أصحابنا فيكون الحكم فيه أنه إن نقص شيء من القدر سقط بقدره شيء من الدين وإلا فلا. وأشار بقوله ثم تخلل إلى أن المرهون عنده مسلم والراهن فلو كان ذمياً قال في المبسوط : رهن ذمي من ذمي خمراً فصارت خلاً لا ينقص من قيمته بقدره ويبقى رهناً لأن بالتغيير من وصف المرارة إلى الحموضة نقصت المالية عندهم ومقومها مع بقاء العين بحالها وبتبدل الصفة لا يبطل الرهن كما لو كان الرهن قلباً فانكسر وبقي الوزن على حاله، ثم عندهما يتخير الراهن إن شاء افتكه بجميع الدين ،وأخذه، وإن شاء ضمنه خمراً مثل خمره فيصير البحر الرائق ٣٣٥/٨ ٥١٤ كتاب الرهن باب التصر في الرهن والجناية عليه وجنايته على غيره الخل سبب يضمن من ملكاً للمرتهن . .. وعند محمد رحمه الله تعالى إن شاء افتكه بجميع الدين وإن شاء جعله بالدين كما في مسئلة القلب إذا انكسر كما مر بيانه وقيدنا بقولنا رهن مسلم عصيراً» لأن رهن الكافر الخمر عند مسلم أو رهن المسلم الخمر عند كافر باطل قال : ارتهن المسلم من كافر خمراً فصار خلاً في الرهن باطل ويكون الخل أمانة في يده للراهن وهو بالخيار إن شاء أخذه قضاء دينه، وإن شاء يدع الخل بدينه إن كانت قيمة الخل يوم الرهن كالدين لأن المسلم يجوز أن يضمن الخمر بالرهن لأنه ضمان والمضمون متى نصب في يد الضمين يخير من له الضمان كما لو غصب المسلم خمراً من ذمي فصارت خلاً في يده يخير الذمي لأن الخمر عند أهل الذمة يصلح لمنافع ما لا يصلح له الخل ولا وجه فصار الخمر كالهالك من وجه، وليس له المرتهن خمراً مثل خمره لأن المسلم منهي عن تمليك الخمر ولا وجه أن يترك الخل عليه ويضمن النقصان لأنه يؤدي إلى الربا ولا وجه أن يأخذ الخل ويضمن الدين كله لأنه يتضرر به فقلنا بأنه يجعله بالدين ليدفع الضرر عنه وليس فيه ضرر على المرتهن فكذا هذا، فإذا وجبت قيمة الخمر للراهن على المرتهن فله عليه مثل ذلك فيلتقيان قصاصاً. ولو ارتهن الكافر خمراً من مسلم لا يجوز ويكون أمانة في يد المرتهن لأن الخمر لا يصير مضموناً على الكافر المسلم وإن قبضها بجهة الضمان كما في الغصب والإتلاف. ارتهن مسلم من مسلم عصيراً فصار خمراً فللمرتهن تخليلها وتكون رهناً، ويبطل من الدين بحساب ما نقص يعني من الكيل والوزن بقذف الزبد لأن . التخليل إحياء حق المرتهن وإصلاح الفاسد فله ذلك، وفي إبقاء العقد بعد التخمير فائدة لجواز التخليل فيبقى كالعصير إذا تخمر قبل القبض يبقى البيع فكذا هذا ، والدين يسقط بانتقاص الرهن لأنه احتبس عنده بعض الرهن ولا ينقص بانتقاص القيمة كما إذا تغير السعر وقيدنا بذكر العصير في المسلم قال وإن كان الراهن كافراً يأخذ الخمر والدين عليه وليس للمرتهن أن يخلها، فإن خللها ضمن قيمتها يوم خلل ورجع بدينه بخلاف ما لو كان الراهن مسلماً فخللها لم يضمن. والفرق أن هناك لا ضرر على الراهن في إبقاء عقد الرهن إلى ما بعد التخمير بل له فيه منفعة لأن ماله يصير متقوماً بالتخليل ولم يصر المرتهن متلفاً لماله بل إضرار بالراهن لأن لأهل رغائب في الخمر ما ليس مثلها في العصير وهو لم يرضى بكون الخمر رهناً، فلو بقينا عقد الرهن بعد تغيير ملكه في حقه يؤدي إلى الضرر به لأن الخمر بالعصير جنسان مختلفان في حق أهل الذمة وهو لم يعقد الرهن على الخمر وإنما عقد على العصير فلا يبقى العقد فيكون للراهن أخذ الخمر من المرتهن، فإن خللها يضمن قيمتها لأنه أتلف الخمر بالتخليل على الذمي لما بينا والله أعلم. رهن ذمي من ذمي جلد ميتة فدبغه المرتهن لم يكن رهناً وأخذه الراهن وأعطاه أجرة الدباغة إن كان له قيمة لأن جلد الميتة ليس بمال عند أحد فلم ينعقد العقد لفوات المحل فلا . جائزاً بحدوث المحلية من بعد كما لو رهن من مسلم خمراً فصارت خلا فإذا دبغه بشيء له قيمة بقي أثره في الجلد فيكون له على صاحب الجلد قيمة ما زاد الدباغ فيه كمن صبغ ثوب انسان الذمة يعود
Chapter of Pledge: Section on Disposition of the Pledge and Crime Upon It and Its Crime Upon Others. A section. He pledged grape juice worth ten for ten, then it became wine, then it became vinegar and it is worth ten, so it is a pledge for ten. And for the other [creditors] to reject it because it is preferring some creditors by a notional fulfillment, so it resembles preference by actual fulfillment. The commonality is the nullification of the right of other creditors. Do you not see that the deceased himself does not own that in his death illness, so likewise the one who stands in his place. If he pays their debt before they reject it, it is permissible because the impediment is removed and their right is fulfilled. If the deceased had no other creditor, the pledge is permissible by analogy to actual fulfillment. And it is sold as it was sold before the pledge, so likewise after. And if the executor pledges for a debt due to the deceased against a man, it is permissible because it is recovery, so he owns it and may sell it, and Allah knows best. His debt because it fails to follow the section. This section is like the scattered [rulings] mentioned at the ends of books, so he delayed it to make up for what he missed earlier. He (the author) said: 'He pledged grape juice worth ten for ten, then it became wine, then it became vinegar and it is worth ten, so it is a pledge for ten' meaning if he pledges grape juice with a Muslim up to its end. They said: what is a valid subject of sale in continuation is a valid subject of pledge in continuation, just as what is a valid subject of sale initially is a valid subject of pledge initially. Wine is a valid subject of sale in continuation even if not initially. I say: one could say if the basis of our mentioned issue were this limited reasoning, there would be no benefit in his saying 'then it became vinegar' in posing the issue; rather it would suffice to say 'and whoever pledges grape juice for ten then it becomes wine, it is a pledge for ten' because the mentioned reasoning would suffice to establish this general meaning. So reflect. The author of al-`Inaya said: one could say what relates to the subject, initial and continuation are the same, so why [differentiate from] that principle? And he said: it can be answered that it is so when the subject remains, and here the subject changes legally by change of description, so it fails to follow that principle. End. I say: his saying 'then it became vinegar and it is worth ten' indicates that what is considered in increase and decrease is value, but it is not so; rather what is considered is quantity because grape juice and vinegar are measured items, being either by weight or measure. In them, decrease in value does not necessitate reduction of any of that debt, as previously in the case of a broken heart; rather it necessitates an option as we mentioned, because the purpose is merely description, and loss of any description in measured items does not necessitate reduction of anything from the debt by consensus of our companions. So the ruling is that if something of the quantity is lost, an equivalent of the debt is reduced, otherwise not. And he indicated by his saying 'then it became vinegar' that the pledgee is a Muslim and the pledgor. If the pledgor were a dhimmi, it is said in al-Mabsut: a dhimmi pledged wine from a dhimmi, then it became vinegar – it does not decrease from its value proportionally and remains a pledge because by change from the description of bitterness to sourness, the monetary value decreased in their estimation, but the substance remains as is, and by change of description the pledge is not invalidated, as if the pledge were a heart that broke while the weight remained. Then according to them, the pledgor has the choice: if he wishes, he may redeem it with the full debt and take it, and if he wishes, he may guarantee wine like his wine, so it becomes... The vinegar becomes the cause for guarantee from the ownership of the pledgee. And according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): if he wishes, he may redeem it with the full debt, and if he wishes, he may make it against the debt as in the case of the heart that broke as previously explained. And we restricted by our saying 'a Muslim pledged grape juice' because the pledge of wine by a kafir to a Muslim or the pledge of wine by a Muslim to a kafir is invalid. He said: a Muslim took wine as pledge from a kafir, then it became vinegar in the pledge – the pledge is invalid, and the vinegar is a trust in his hand for the pledgor, and he has the choice: if he wishes, he may take it as payment of his debt, and if he wishes, he may leave the vinegar for his debt if the value of the vinegar on the day of pledge equals the debt, because a Muslim is permitted to guarantee wine by pledge since it is a guarantee, and when the guaranteed thing is placed in the hand of the guarantor, the one entitled to the guarantee has the choice, just as if a Muslim seized wine from a dhimmi and it became vinegar in his hand – the dhimmi has the choice because wine for the dhimmis is suitable for benefits for which vinegar is not suitable, and there is no way, so wine became like destroyed in one sense. And he does not have wine like his wine because the Muslim is forbidden from making wine property, and there is no way to leave the vinegar on it and guarantee the decrease because it leads to usury, and there is no way to take the vinegar and guarantee the full debt because it harms him. So we said he makes it against the debt to remove harm from him, and there is no harm on the pledgee, so likewise this. So when the value of the wine becomes due to the pledgor on the pledgee, he has similar against him, so they offset. If the kafir took wine as pledge from a Muslim, it is invalid, and it is a trust in the hand of the pledgee because wine does not become guaranteed against the Muslim kafir even if he takes it by the way of guarantee, as in usurpation and destruction. A Muslim pledged grape juice from a Muslim, then it became wine – the pledgee may turn it into vinegar, and it remains a pledge, and the debt is reduced by the amount of what decreased in terms of measure/weight by throwing out the foam because turning into vinegar is reviving the right of the pledgee and repairing what is corrupt, so he has that right. And there is benefit in keeping the contract after fermentation for the permissibility of turning into vinegar, so it remains like grape juice that fermented before taking possession – the sale remains, so likewise this. The debt is reduced by the decrease of the pledge because some of the pledge is detained with him, and it does not decrease by decrease of value as when the price changes. And we restricted by mentioning grape juice in the Muslim. He said: if the pledgor is a kafir, he takes the wine and the debt remains upon him, and the pledgee may not turn it into vinegar. If he turns it into vinegar, he guarantees its value on the day he turned it and returns to his debt, unlike if the pledgor were Muslim and he turned it into vinegar – he does not guarantee. The difference is that there is no harm on the pledgor in keeping the pledge contract after fermentation; rather he has benefit because his property becomes valuable by turning into vinegar, and the pledgee did not become a destroyer of his property, but harm to the pledgor because the people have desires in wine that are not like in grape juice, and he did not consent to the wine being a pledge. If we kept the pledge contract after changing his property in his right, it leads to harm to him because wine and grape juice are two different types in the right of the dhimmis, and he did not contract the pledge on wine, but on grape juice, so the contract does not remain. So the pledgor may take the wine from the pledgee. If he turns it into vinegar, he guarantees its value because he destroyed the wine by turning to vinegar upon the dhimmi as we explained, and Allah knows best. A dhimmi pledged a dead animal skin from a dhimmi, then the pledgee tanned it – it is not a pledge, and the pledgor takes it and gives him the wage of tanning if it has value, because the skin of a dead animal is not property for anyone, so the contract did not conclude due to lack of subject. It does not become valid by subsequent acquisition of subject, as if he pledged wine from a Muslim and it became vinegar. So if he tanned it with something of value, its effect remains in the skin, so he has upon the owner of the skin the value of what the tanning increased, like one who dyed the garment of a person.. the dhimma returns.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the rulings regarding changes in the state of a pledged item and cross-religious pledges. It begins with the main ruling: if grape juice worth ten is pledged for ten, then becomes wine, then vinegar, it remains a pledge for ten. The commentator explains that the key is whether the item is a valid subject of sale in continuation; wine is valid for sale after fermentation. A subtle point is raised: the consideration for increase/decrease is quantity (measure/weight), not value, because grape juice and vinegar are measured items. The passage then discusses several scenarios: (1) a dhimmi pledging wine to a dhimmi – if it becomes vinegar, the pledge remains, but the pledgor has options; (2) a Muslim taking wine as pledge from a kafir – the pledge is invalid, and the vinegar is a trust; (3) a kafir taking wine from a Muslim – invalid; (4) a Muslim pledging grape juice to a Muslim – if it becomes wine, the pledgee may turn it to vinegar, and the debt reduces by the quantity loss; (5) a dhimmi pledging a dead animal skin – the pledge is invalid even if tanned, but the tanner gets compensation. Throughout, the commentator uses qiyas and istihsan, distinguishing between cases based on the religious status of the parties and the nature of the items.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of an Islamic state, protected by covenant.
قلب منكسر (qalb munakassir): A broken heart (from butchery), used as an analogy for a pledged item that changes description while weight remains.
عندهما (indahuma): In the view of the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dhimmi-dhimmi wine pledge case, according to the two disciples (عندهما), what options does the pledgor have?
A) Only to redeem by paying the full debt and take the vinegar.
B) Redeem with full debt or demand wine compensation.
C) Redeem or make the vinegar full payment.
D) Only to demand compensation.

Key Takeaway
When a dhimmi pledges wine that becomes vinegar, the pledge remains valid, and the pledgor has options (with ikhtilaf); the mufta bihi follows the two disciples.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Cross-Religious Pledges – Muslim and Dhimmi: Invalidity and Consequences
Detailed rulings on invalidity of cross-religious wine pledges and post-transformation remedies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which cross-religious pledges are invalid and state the consequences when wine turns to vinegar in such cases.

Core Idea
A pledge involving wine is invalid if one party is Muslim; if wine becomes vinegar in such a pledge, the vinegar is a trust, and parties have limited remedies.

This lesson covers scenarios where a Muslim and a dhimmi are parties to a pledge involving wine. The Hanafi school rules these pledges invalid (batil) because wine is not property for Muslims and cannot be a valid subject of a contract with a Muslim. When the wine later becomes vinegar, the vinegar is considered a trust (amanah) in the hands of the Muslim pledgee, and the former pledgor has choices to resolve the debt. The lesson also contrasts the invalid cross-religious cases with the valid Muslim-Muslim and dhimmi-dhimmi cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two invalid scenarios
Scenario A: Muslim pledgee takes wine from a kafir pledgor. Scenario B: Kafir pledgee takes wine from a Muslim pledgor. Both are invalid because wine is non-mal for Muslims and cannot be the subject of a contract with a Muslim party.
The text explicitly states these are batil.
2
State the ruling for Scenario A (Muslim pledgee, kafir pledgor)
The pledge is invalid (batil). If the wine becomes vinegar in the Muslim's possession, the vinegar is a trust (amanah) in his hand. The pledgor (kafir) has a choice: either take the vinegar as full payment for the debt (if value equals debt) or leave it as security for the debt.
This is based on istihsan to avoid harm to both parties.
3
Explain the reasoning for Scenario A
The Muslim cannot own wine, but he can guarantee it, similar to a guarantor. By analogy to a Muslim who seizes wine from a dhimmi and it becomes vinegar, the dhimmi has a choice. The debt becomes offset because the wine's value is due from one side and the vinegar's value from the other.
The text uses the analogy of ghasp (usurpation) of wine.
4
State the ruling for Scenario B (kafir pledgee, Muslim pledgor)
The pledge is invalid (batil). The wine in the hands of the kafir pledgee is a trust. The pledgee cannot own the wine, and the pledgor remains responsible for the debt. If the wine becomes vinegar, the pledgee must return it; the debt remains.
Wine does not become guaranteed against the Muslim pledgor.
5
Contrast with the valid Muslim-Muslim and dhimmi-dhimmi cases
When both parties are same religion and the subject is initially permissible for them, the pledge is valid. The invalidity arises solely from mixing religious capacities with a non-mal subject.
The restriction (qayd) of 'Muslim' in earlier phrasing is crucial.

كتاب الرهن باب التصرف في الرهن والجناية عليه وجنايته على غيره فصل رهن عصيراً قيمته عشرة بعشرة فتخمر ثم تخلل وهو يساوي عشرة فهو رهن بعشرة وللآخر أن يردوه لأنه إيثار لبعض الغرماء بالإيفاء الحكمي فأشبهه الإيثار بالإيفاء الحقيقي، والجامع ما في كل واحد منهما من إبطال حق غيره من الغرماء ألا ترى أن الميت بنفسه لا يملك ذلك بمرض موته فكذا من قام مقامه . وإن قضى دينهم قبل أن يردوه جاز لزوال المانع ووصول حقهم، ولو لم يكن للميت غريم آخر جاز الرهن اعتباراً بالإيفاء الحقيقي وبيع في يباع فيه قبل الرهن فكذا بعده، وإذا ارتهن الوصي بدين للميت على رجل جاز لأنه استيفاء فيملكه وله أن يبيعه والله أعلم. دينه لأنه تخلف عن فصل هذا الفصل بمنزلة المتفرقة المذكورة في أواخر الكتب فلذا أخره استدراكاً لما فاته فيما سبق . قال رحمه الله : رهن عصيراً قيمته عشرة بعشرة فتخمر ثم تخلل وهو يساوي عشرة فهو رهن بعشرة) يعني إذا رهن عند مسلم عصيراً إلى آخرها قالوا ما كان محلاً للبيع بقاء يكون محلاً للرهن بقاء كما أن ما يكون محلاً للبيع ابتداء يكون محلاً للرهن ابتداء والخمر محل للبيع بقاء وإن لم يكن محلاً له ابتداء ، أقول : وههنا يتبدل المحل حكماً بتبدل الوصف فكذلك تخلف عن ذلك الأصل ا هـ . . أقول : قوله : «ثم تخلل وهي تساوي عشرة» يشير إلى أن المعتبر فيه في الزيادة والنقصان القيمة وليس كذلك بل المعتبر القدر لأن العصير مسلم عصيراً» لأن رهن الكافر الخمر عند مسلم أو رهن المسلم الخمر عند كافر باطل قال : ارتهن المسلم من كافر خمراً فصار خلاً في الرهن باطل ويكون الخل أمانة في يده للراهن وهو بالخيار إن شاء أخذه قضاء دينه، وإن شاء يدع الخل بدينه إن كانت قيمة الخل يوم الرهن كالدين لأن المسلم يجوز أن يضمن الخمر بالرهن لأنه ضمان والمضمون متى نصب في يد الضمين يخير من له الضمان كما لو غصب المسلم خمراً من ذمي فصارت خلاً في يده يخير الذمي لأن الخمر عند أهل الذمة يصلح لمنافع ما لا يصلح له الخل ولا وجه فصار الخمر كالهالك من وجه، وليس له المرتهن خمراً مثل خمره لأن المسلم منهي عن تمليك الخمر ولا وجه أن يترك الخل عليه ويضمن النقصان لأنه يؤدي إلى الربا ولا وجه أن يأخذ الخل ويضمن الدين كله لأنه يتضرر به فقلنا بأنه يجعله بالدين ليدفع الضرر عنه وليس فيه ضرر على المرتهن فكذا هذا، فإذا وجبت قيمة الخمر للراهن على المرتهن فله عليه مثل ذلك فيلتقيان قصاصاً. ولو ارتهن الكافر خمراً من مسلم لا يجوز ويكون أمانة في يد المرتهن لأن الخمر لا يصير مضموناً على الكافر المسلم وإن قبضها بجهة الضمان كما في الغصب والإتلاف. ارتهن مسلم من مسلم عصيراً فصار خمراً فللمرتهن تخليلها وتكون رهناً، ويبطل من الدين بحساب ما نقص يعني من الكيل والوزن بقذف الزبد لأن . التخليل إحياء حق المرتهن وإصلاح الفاسد فله ذلك، وفي إبقاء العقد بعد التخمير فائدة لجواز التخليل فيبقى كالعصير إذا تخمر قبل القبض يبقى البيع فكذا هذا ، والدين يسقط بانتقاص الرهن لأنه احتبس عنده بعض الرهن ولا ينقص بانتقاص القيمة كما إذا تغير السعر وقيدنا بذكر العصير في المسلم قال وإن كان الراهن كافراً يأخذ الخمر والدين عليه وليس للمرتهن أن يخلها، فإن خللها ضمن قيمتها يوم خلل ورجع بدينه بخلاف ما لو كان الراهن مسلماً فخللها لم يضمن. والفرق أن هناك لا ضرر على الراهن في إبقاء عقد الرهن إلى ما بعد التخمير بل له فيه منفعة لأن ماله يصير متقوماً بالتخليل ولم يصر المرتهن متلفاً لماله بل إضرار بالراهن لأن لأهل رغائب في الخمر ما ليس مثلها في العصير وهو لم يرضى بكون الخمر رهناً، فلو بقينا عقد الرهن بعد تغيير ملكه في حقه يؤدي إلى الضرر به لأن الخمر بالعصير جنسان مختلفان في حق أهل الذمة وهو لم يعقد الرهن على الخمر وإنما عقد على العصير فلا يبقى العقد فيكون للراهن أخذ الخمر من المرتهن، فإن خللها يضمن قيمتها لأنه أتلف الخمر بالتخليل على الذمي لما بينا والله أعلم. رهن ذمي من ذمي جلد ميتة فدبغه المرتهن لم يكن رهناً وأخذه الراهن وأعطاه أجرة الدباغة إن كان له قيمة لأن جلد الميتة ليس بمال عند أحد فلم ينعقد العقد لفوات المحل فلا . جائزاً بحدوث المحلية من بعد كما لو رهن من مسلم خمراً فصارت خلا فإذا دبغه بشيء له قيمة بقي أثره في الجلد فيكون له على صاحب الجلد قيمة ما زاد الدباغ فيه كمن صبغ ثوب انسان الذمة يعود
Chapter of Pledge: Section on Disposition of the Pledge and Crime Upon It and Its Crime Upon Others. A section. He pledged grape juice worth ten for ten, then it became wine, then it became vinegar and it is worth ten, so it is a pledge for ten. And for the other [creditors] to reject it because it is preferring some creditors by a notional fulfillment, so it resembles preference by actual fulfillment. The commonality is the nullification of the right of other creditors. Do you not see that the deceased himself does not own that in his death illness, so likewise the one who stands in his place. If he pays their debt before they reject it, it is permissible because the impediment is removed and their right is fulfilled. If the deceased had no other creditor, the pledge is permissible by analogy to actual fulfillment. And it is sold as it was sold before the pledge, so likewise after. And if the executor pledges for a debt due to the deceased against a man, it is permissible because it is recovery, so he owns it and may sell it, and Allah knows best. His debt because it fails to follow the section. This section is like the scattered [rulings] mentioned at the ends of books, so he delayed it to make up for what he missed earlier. He (the author) said: 'He pledged grape juice worth ten for ten, then it became wine, then it became vinegar and it is worth ten, so it is a pledge for ten' meaning if he pledges grape juice with a Muslim up to its end. They said: what is a valid subject of sale in continuation is a valid subject of pledge in continuation, just as what is a valid subject of sale initially is a valid subject of pledge initially. Wine is a valid subject of sale in continuation even if not initially. I say: one could say if the basis of our mentioned issue were this limited reasoning, there would be no benefit in his saying 'then it became vinegar' in posing the issue; rather it would suffice to say 'and whoever pledges grape juice for ten then it becomes wine, it is a pledge for ten' because the mentioned reasoning would suffice to establish this general meaning. So reflect. The author of al-`Inaya said: one could say what relates to the subject, initial and continuation are the same, so why [differentiate from] that principle? And he said: it can be answered that it is so when the subject remains, and here the subject changes legally by change of description, so it fails to follow that principle. End. I say: his saying 'then it became vinegar and it is worth ten' indicates that what is considered in increase and decrease is value, but it is not so; rather what is considered is quantity because grape juice and vinegar are measured items, being either by weight or measure. In them, decrease in value does not necessitate reduction of any of that debt, as previously in the case of a broken heart; rather it necessitates an option as we mentioned, because the purpose is merely description, and loss of any description in measured items does not necessitate reduction of anything from the debt by consensus of our companions. So the ruling is that if something of the quantity is lost, an equivalent of the debt is reduced, otherwise not. And he indicated by his saying 'then it became vinegar' that the pledgee is a Muslim and the pledgor. If the pledgor were a dhimmi, it is said in al-Mabsut: a dhimmi pledged wine from a dhimmi, then it became vinegar – it does not decrease from its value proportionally and remains a pledge because by change from the description of bitterness to sourness, the monetary value decreased in their estimation, but the substance remains as is, and by change of description the pledge is not invalidated, as if the pledge were a heart that broke while the weight remained. Then according to them, the pledgor has the choice: if he wishes, he may redeem it with the full debt and take it, and if he wishes, he may guarantee wine like his wine, so it becomes... The vinegar becomes the cause for guarantee from the ownership of the pledgee. And according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): if he wishes, he may redeem it with the full debt, and if he wishes, he may make it against the debt as in the case of the heart that broke as previously explained. And we restricted by our saying 'a Muslim pledged grape juice' because the pledge of wine by a kafir to a Muslim or the pledge of wine by a Muslim to a kafir is invalid. He said: a Muslim took wine as pledge from a kafir, then it became vinegar in the pledge – the pledge is invalid, and the vinegar is a trust in his hand for the pledgor, and he has the choice: if he wishes, he may take it as payment of his debt, and if he wishes, he may leave the vinegar for his debt if the value of the vinegar on the day of pledge equals the debt, because a Muslim is permitted to guarantee wine by pledge since it is a guarantee, and when the guaranteed thing is placed in the hand of the guarantor, the one entitled to the guarantee has the choice, just as if a Muslim seized wine from a dhimmi and it became vinegar in his hand – the dhimmi has the choice because wine for the dhimmis is suitable for benefits for which vinegar is not suitable, and there is no way, so wine became like destroyed in one sense. And he does not have wine like his wine because the Muslim is forbidden from making wine property, and there is no way to leave the vinegar on it and guarantee the decrease because it leads to usury, and there is no way to take the vinegar and guarantee the full debt because it harms him. So we said he makes it against the debt to remove harm from him, and there is no harm on the pledgee, so likewise this. So when the value of the wine becomes due to the pledgor on the pledgee, he has similar against him, so they offset. If the kafir took wine as pledge from a Muslim, it is invalid, and it is a trust in the hand of the pledgee because wine does not become guaranteed against the Muslim kafir even if he takes it by the way of guarantee, as in usurpation and destruction. A Muslim pledged grape juice from a Muslim, then it became wine – the pledgee may turn it into vinegar, and it remains a pledge, and the debt is reduced by the amount of what decreased in terms of measure/weight by throwing out the foam because turning into vinegar is reviving the right of the pledgee and repairing what is corrupt, so he has that right. And there is benefit in keeping the contract after fermentation for the permissibility of turning into vinegar, so it remains like grape juice that fermented before taking possession – the sale remains, so likewise this. The debt is reduced by the decrease of the pledge because some of the pledge is detained with him, and it does not decrease by decrease of value as when the price changes. And we restricted by mentioning grape juice in the Muslim. He said: if the pledgor is a kafir, he takes the wine and the debt remains upon him, and the pledgee may not turn it into vinegar. If he turns it into vinegar, he guarantees its value on the day he turned it and returns to his debt, unlike if the pledgor were Muslim and he turned it into vinegar – he does not guarantee. The difference is that there is no harm on the pledgor in keeping the pledge contract after fermentation; rather he has benefit because his property becomes valuable by turning into vinegar, and the pledgee did not become a destroyer of his property, but harm to the pledgor because the people have desires in wine that are not like in grape juice, and he did not consent to the wine being a pledge. If we kept the pledge contract after changing his property in his right, it leads to harm to him because wine and grape juice are two different types in the right of the dhimmis, and he did not contract the pledge on wine, but on grape juice, so the contract does not remain. So the pledgor may take the wine from the pledgee. If he turns it into vinegar, he guarantees its value because he destroyed the wine by turning to vinegar upon the dhimmi as we explained, and Allah knows best. A dhimmi pledged a dead animal skin from a dhimmi, then the pledgee tanned it – it is not a pledge, and the pledgor takes it and gives him the wage of tanning if it has value, because the skin of a dead animal is not property for anyone, so the contract did not conclude due to lack of subject. It does not become valid by subsequent acquisition of subject, as if he pledged wine from a Muslim and it became vinegar. So if he tanned it with something of value, its effect remains in the skin, so he has upon the owner of the skin the value of what the tanning increased, like one who dyed the garment of a person.. the dhimma returns.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the rulings regarding changes in the state of a pledged item and cross-religious pledges. It begins with the main ruling: if grape juice worth ten is pledged for ten, then becomes wine, then vinegar, it remains a pledge for ten. The commentator explains that the key is whether the item is a valid subject of sale in continuation; wine is valid for sale after fermentation. A subtle point is raised: the consideration for increase/decrease is quantity (measure/weight), not value, because grape juice and vinegar are measured items. The passage then discusses several scenarios: (1) a dhimmi pledging wine to a dhimmi – if it becomes vinegar, the pledge remains, but the pledgor has options; (2) a Muslim taking wine as pledge from a kafir – the pledge is invalid, and the vinegar is a trust; (3) a kafir taking wine from a Muslim – invalid; (4) a Muslim pledging grape juice to a Muslim – if it becomes wine, the pledgee may turn it to vinegar, and the debt reduces by the quantity loss; (5) a dhimmi pledging a dead animal skin – the pledge is invalid even if tanned, but the tanner gets compensation. Throughout, the commentator uses qiyas and istihsan, distinguishing between cases based on the religious status of the parties and the nature of the items.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; the Muslim becomes a guarantor (damin) for the wine's value.
غصب (ghasp): Usurpation; wrongful seizure of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A Muslim takes wine as a pledge from a Christian (dhimmi). The wine turns into vinegar. What is the status of the vinegar?
A) It becomes the property of the Muslim pledgee.
B) It is a trust in the Muslim's hands; the Christian may take it as payment or leave it.
C) It must be destroyed immediately.
D) The pledgee may sell it and recover the debt.

Key Takeaway
Pledges involving wine are invalid when a Muslim party is involved; the transformation to vinegar does not validate it, but creates a trust with limited remedies.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
9 min
Pledgee's Rights to Transform and Invalid Subjects – Muslim Pledgee Turning Wine to Vinegar and Dead Animal Skin
Two ruling units: pledgee's right to transform wine to vinegar and invalidity of dead animal skin pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a Muslim pledgee's right to turn pledged wine into vinegar, and explain why a pledge of a dead animal skin (maytah) is invalid even after tanning.

Core Idea
A Muslim pledgee may turn pledged wine into vinegar (since vinegar is beneficial), but cannot do so if the pledgor is a dhimmi; a dead animal skin is non-property, so its pledge is void even after tanning.

This lesson covers two distinct rulings from the passage. First, when a Muslim pledges grape juice to a Muslim and it becomes wine, the pledgee has the right to turn it into vinegar, reviving the pledge's value. This is based on istihsan: it is repairing a corrupt situation. Second, the passage addresses the pledge of a dead animal skin (maytah) by a dhimmi to a dhimmi. Since a dead animal skin is not property (mal) for anyone, the pledge is void ab initio, even if the pledgee tans it. The tanner is entitled to compensation for his work, but not ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first case: Muslim-to-Muslim pledge of grape juice that becomes wine
The pledgor and pledgee are both Muslims; the subject is grape juice that becomes wine (non-mal for Muslims). The pledge contract remains valid because it was valid initially.
This is the continuation of Lesson 1's scenario.
2
Rule: Pledgee may turn wine into vinegar
The pledgee has the right to turn the wine into vinegar (takhleel). This is considered 'reviving a right' and 'repairing the corrupt'. By doing so, the pledge remains valid and the debt is reduced by the quantity lost (e.g., foam).
The text analogizes to the case of grape juice that ferments before possession – the sale remains.
3
Identify the second case: Dhimmi pledging dead animal skin
A dhimmi pledges the skin of a dead animal (maytah) to another dhimmi. The subject is not property (mal) according to any school, so the contract is void from the start.
A dead animal skin is najas and non-property until tanned, but the tanning does not retroactively validate the pledge.
4
Rule: Pledge invalid; tanner gets compensation
The pledge is invalid. If the pledgee tans the skin, the pledgor may take it back, but must pay the pledgee the wage for tanning (ajrat al-dibaghah) because the tanning added value. This is like the case of dyeing a garment.
The tanning does not create property rights; the tanner has a claim for his work.
5
Distinguish the pledgee's actions in the two cases
In the first case, the pledgee may transform the subject (wine to vinegar) because it preserves the pledge and benefits the pledgor. In the second case, the pledgee cannot acquire the subject through transformation (tanning); he only gets compensation.
The difference lies in the initial validity of the contract.

كتاب الرهن باب التصرف في الرهن والجناية عليه وجنايته على غيره فصل رهن عصيراً قيمته عشرة بعشرة فتخمر ثم تخلل وهو يساوي عشرة فهو رهن بعشرة وللآخر أن يردوه لأنه إيثار لبعض الغرماء بالإيفاء الحكمي فأشبهه الإيثار بالإيفاء الحقيقي، والجامع ما في كل واحد منهما من إبطال حق غيره من الغرماء ألا ترى أن الميت بنفسه لا يملك ذلك بمرض موته فكذا من قام مقامه . وإن قضى دينهم قبل أن يردوه جاز لزوال المانع ووصول حقهم، ولو لم يكن للميت غريم آخر جاز الرهن اعتباراً بالإيفاء الحقيقي وبيع في يباع فيه قبل الرهن فكذا بعده، وإذا ارتهن الوصي بدين للميت على رجل جاز لأنه استيفاء فيملكه وله أن يبيعه والله أعلم. دينه لأنه تخلف عن فصل هذا الفصل بمنزلة المتفرقة المذكورة في أواخر الكتب فلذا أخره استدراكاً لما فاته فيما سبق . قال رحمه الله : رهن عصيراً قيمته عشرة بعشرة فتخمر ثم تخلل وهو يساوي عشرة فهو رهن بعشرة) يعني إذا وههنا يتبدل المحل حكماً بتبدل الوصف فكذلك تخلف عن ذلك الأصل ا هـ . . أقول : قوله : «ثم تخلل وهي تساوي عشرة» يشير إلى أن المعتبر فيه في الزيادة والنقصان القيمة وليس كذلك بل المعتبر القدر لأن العصير والخل من المقدرات لأنه إما مكيل أو موزون وفيها نقصان القيمة لا يوجب سقوط شيء من ذلك الدين كما مر في انكسار القلب، وإنما يوجب الخيار على ما ذكرنا لأن الغاية فيه مجرد الوصف وفوات كل شيء من الوصف في مسلم عصيراً» لأن رهن الكافر الخمر عند مسلم أو رهن المسلم الخمر عند كافر باطل قال : ارتهن المسلم من كافر خمراً فصار خلاً في الرهن باطل ويكون الخل أمانة في يده للراهن وهو بالخيار إن شاء أخذه قضاء دينه، وإن شاء يدع الخل بدينه إن كانت قيمة الخل يوم الرهن كالدين لأن المسلم يجوز أن يضمن الخمر بالرهن لأنه ضمان والمضمون متى نصب في يد الضمين يخير من له الضمان كما لو غصب المسلم خمراً من ذمي فصارت خلاً في يده يخير الذمي لأن الخمر عند أهل الذمة يصلح لمنافع ما لا يصلح له الخل ولا وجه فصار الخمر كالهالك من وجه، وليس له المرتهن خمراً مثل خمره لأن المسلم منهي عن تمليك الخمر ولا وجه أن يترك الخل عليه ويضمن النقصان لأنه يؤدي إلى الربا ولا وجه أن يأخذ الخل ويضمن الدين كله لأنه يتضرر به فقلنا بأنه يجعله بالدين ليدفع الضرر عنه وليس فيه ضرر على المرتهن فكذا هذا، فإذا وجبت قيمة الخمر للراهن على المرتهن فله عليه مثل ذلك فيلتقيان قصاصاً. ولو ارتهن الكافر خمراً من مسلم لا يجوز ويكون أمانة في يد المرتهن لأن الخمر لا يصير مضموناً على الكافر المسلم وإن قبضها بجهة الضمان كما في الغصب والإتلاف. ارتهن مسلم من مسلم عصيراً فصار خمراً فللمرتهن تخليلها وتكون رهناً، ويبطل من الدين بحساب ما نقص يعني من الكيل والوزن بقذف الزبد لأن . التخليل إحياء حق المرتهن وإصلاح الفاسد فله ذلك، وفي إبقاء العقد بعد التخمير فائدة لجواز التخليل فيبقى كالعصير إذا تخمر قبل القبض يبقى البيع فكذا هذا ، والدين يسقط بانتقاص الرهن لأنه احتبس عنده بعض الرهن ولا ينقص بانتقاص القيمة كما إذا تغير السعر وقيدنا بذكر العصير في المسلم قال وإن كان الراهن كافراً يأخذ الخمر والدين عليه وليس للمرتهن أن يخلها، فإن خللها ضمن قيمتها يوم خلل ورجع بدينه بخلاف ما لو كان الراهن مسلماً فخللها لم يضمن. والفرق أن هناك لا ضرر على الراهن في إبقاء عقد الرهن إلى ما بعد التخمير بل له فيه منفعة لأن ماله يصير متقوماً بالتخليل ولم يصر المرتهن متلفاً لماله بل إضرار بالراهن لأن لأهل رغائب في الخمر ما ليس مثلها في العصير وهو لم يرضى بكون الخمر رهناً، فلو بقينا عقد الرهن بعد تغيير ملكه في حقه يؤدي إلى الضرر به لأن الخمر بالعصير جنسان مختلفان في حق أهل الذمة وهو لم يعقد الرهن على الخمر وإنما عقد على العصير فلا يبقى العقد فيكون للراهن أخذ الخمر من المرتهن، فإن خللها يضمن قيمتها لأنه أتلف الخمر بالتخليل على الذمي لما بينا والله أعلم. رهن ذمي من ذمي جلد ميتة فدبغه المرتهن لم يكن رهناً وأخذه الراهن وأعطاه أجرة الدباغة إن كان له قيمة لأن جلد الميتة ليس بمال عند أحد فلم ينعقد العقد لفوات المحل فلا . جائزاً بحدوث المحلية من بعد كما لو رهن من مسلم خمراً فصارت خلا فإذا دبغه بشيء له قيمة بقي أثره في الجلد فيكون له على صاحب الجلد قيمة ما زاد الدباغ فيه كمن صبغ ثوب انسان الذمة يعود
Chapter of Pledge: Section on Disposition of the Pledge and Crime Upon It and Its Crime Upon Others. A section. He pledged grape juice worth ten for ten, then it became wine, then it became vinegar and it is worth ten, so it is a pledge for ten. And for the other [creditors] to reject it because it is preferring some creditors by a notional fulfillment, so it resembles preference by actual fulfillment. The commonality is the nullification of the right of other creditors. Do you not see that the deceased himself does not own that in his death illness, so likewise the one who stands in his place. If he pays their debt before they reject it, it is permissible because the impediment is removed and their right is fulfilled. If the deceased had no other creditor, the pledge is permissible by analogy to actual fulfillment. And it is sold as it was sold before the pledge, so likewise after. And if the executor pledges for a debt due to the deceased against a man, it is permissible because it is recovery, so he owns it and may sell it, and Allah knows best. His debt because it fails to follow the section. This section is like the scattered [rulings] mentioned at the ends of books, so he delayed it to make up for what he missed earlier. He (the author) said: 'He pledged grape juice worth ten for ten, then it became wine, then it became vinegar and it is worth ten, so it is a pledge for ten' meaning if he pledges grape juice with a Muslim up to its end. They said: what is a valid subject of sale in continuation is a valid subject of pledge in continuation, just as what is a valid subject of sale initially is a valid subject of pledge initially. Wine is a valid subject of sale in continuation even if not initially. I say: one could say if the basis of our mentioned issue were this limited reasoning, there would be no benefit in his saying 'then it became vinegar' in posing the issue; rather it would suffice to say 'and whoever pledges grape juice for ten then it becomes wine, it is a pledge for ten' because the mentioned reasoning would suffice to establish this general meaning. So reflect. The author of al-`Inaya said: one could say what relates to the subject, initial and continuation are the same, so why [differentiate from] that principle? And he said: it can be answered that it is so when the subject remains, and here the subject changes legally by change of description, so it fails to follow that principle. End. I say: his saying 'then it became vinegar and it is worth ten' indicates that what is considered in increase and decrease is value, but it is not so; rather what is considered is quantity because grape juice and vinegar are measured items, being either by weight or measure. In them, decrease in value does not necessitate reduction of any of that debt, as previously in the case of a broken heart; rather it necessitates an option as we mentioned, because the purpose is merely description, and loss of any description in measured items does not necessitate reduction of anything from the debt by consensus of our companions. So the ruling is that if something of the quantity is lost, an equivalent of the debt is reduced, otherwise not. And he indicated by his saying 'then it became vinegar' that the pledgee is a Muslim and the pledgor. If the pledgor were a dhimmi, it is said in al-Mabsut: a dhimmi pledged wine from a dhimmi, then it became vinegar – it does not decrease from its value proportionally and remains a pledge because by change from the description of bitterness to sourness, the monetary value decreased in their estimation, but the substance remains as is, and by change of description the pledge is not invalidated, as if the pledge were a heart that broke while the weight remained. Then according to them, the pledgor has the choice: if he wishes, he may redeem it with the full debt and take it, and if he wishes, he may guarantee wine like his wine, so it becomes... The vinegar becomes the cause for guarantee from the ownership of the pledgee. And according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): if he wishes, he may redeem it with the full debt, and if he wishes, he may make it against the debt as in the case of the heart that broke as previously explained. And we restricted by our saying 'a Muslim pledged grape juice' because the pledge of wine by a kafir to a Muslim or the pledge of wine by a Muslim to a kafir is invalid. He said: a Muslim took wine as pledge from a kafir, then it became vinegar in the pledge – the pledge is invalid, and the vinegar is a trust in his hand for the pledgor, and he has the choice: if he wishes, he may take it as payment of his debt, and if he wishes, he may leave the vinegar for his debt if the value of the vinegar on the day of pledge equals the debt, because a Muslim is permitted to guarantee wine by pledge since it is a guarantee, and when the guaranteed thing is placed in the hand of the guarantor, the one entitled to the guarantee has the choice, just as if a Muslim seized wine from a dhimmi and it became vinegar in his hand – the dhimmi has the choice because wine for the dhimmis is suitable for benefits for which vinegar is not suitable, and there is no way, so wine became like destroyed in one sense. And he does not have wine like his wine because the Muslim is forbidden from making wine property, and there is no way to leave the vinegar on it and guarantee the decrease because it leads to usury, and there is no way to take the vinegar and guarantee the full debt because it harms him. So we said he makes it against the debt to remove harm from him, and there is no harm on the pledgee, so likewise this. So when the value of the wine becomes due to the pledgor on the pledgee, he has similar against him, so they offset. If the kafir took wine as pledge from a Muslim, it is invalid, and it is a trust in the hand of the pledgee because wine does not become guaranteed against the Muslim kafir even if he takes it by the way of guarantee, as in usurpation and destruction. A Muslim pledged grape juice from a Muslim, then it became wine – the pledgee may turn it into vinegar, and it remains a pledge, and the debt is reduced by the amount of what decreased in terms of measure/weight by throwing out the foam because turning into vinegar is reviving the right of the pledgee and repairing what is corrupt, so he has that right. And there is benefit in keeping the contract after fermentation for the permissibility of turning into vinegar, so it remains like grape juice that fermented before taking possession – the sale remains, so likewise this. The debt is reduced by the decrease of the pledge because some of the pledge is detained with him, and it does not decrease by decrease of value as when the price changes. And we restricted by mentioning grape juice in the Muslim. He said: if the pledgor is a kafir, he takes the wine and the debt remains upon him, and the pledgee may not turn it into vinegar. If he turns it into vinegar, he guarantees its value on the day he turned it and returns to his debt, unlike if the pledgor were Muslim and he turned it into vinegar – he does not guarantee. The difference is that there is no harm on the pledgor in keeping the pledge contract after fermentation; rather he has benefit because his property becomes valuable by turning into vinegar, and the pledgee did not become a destroyer of his property, but harm to the pledgor because the people have desires in wine that are not like in grape juice, and he did not consent to the wine being a pledge. If we kept the pledge contract after changing his property in his right, it leads to harm to him because wine and grape juice are two different types in the right of the dhimmis, and he did not contract the pledge on wine, but on grape juice, so the contract does not remain. So the pledgor may take the wine from the pledgee. If he turns it into vinegar, he guarantees its value because he destroyed the wine by turning to vinegar upon the dhimmi as we explained, and Allah knows best. A dhimmi pledged a dead animal skin from a dhimmi, then the pledgee tanned it – it is not a pledge, and the pledgor takes it and gives him the wage of tanning if it has value, because the skin of a dead animal is not property for anyone, so the contract did not conclude due to lack of subject. It does not become valid by subsequent acquisition of subject, as if he pledged wine from a Muslim and it became vinegar. So if he tanned it with something of value, its effect remains in the skin, so he has upon the owner of the skin the value of what the tanning increased, like one who dyed the garment of a person.. the dhimma returns.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the rulings regarding changes in the state of a pledged item and cross-religious pledges. It begins with the main ruling: if grape juice worth ten is pledged for ten, then becomes wine, then vinegar, it remains a pledge for ten. The commentator explains that the key is whether the item is a valid subject of sale in continuation; wine is valid for sale after fermentation. A subtle point is raised: the consideration for increase/decrease is quantity (measure/weight), not value, because grape juice and vinegar are measured items. The passage then discusses several scenarios: (1) a dhimmi pledging wine to a dhimmi – if it becomes vinegar, the pledge remains, but the pledgor has options; (2) a Muslim taking wine as pledge from a kafir – the pledge is invalid, and the vinegar is a trust; (3) a kafir taking wine from a Muslim – invalid; (4) a Muslim pledging grape juice to a Muslim – if it becomes wine, the pledgee may turn it to vinegar, and the debt reduces by the quantity loss; (5) a dhimmi pledging a dead animal skin – the pledge is invalid even if tanned, but the tanner gets compensation. Throughout, the commentator uses qiyas and istihsan, distinguishing between cases based on the religious status of the parties and the nature of the items.

Key Terms
تخليل (takhleel): The process of turning wine into vinegar, making it halal and valuable.
أجرة الدباغة (ujrat al-dibaghah): Wage for tanning, compensation for the tanner's labor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A Muslim pledges grape juice to a Muslim; it becomes wine. May the pledgee turn it into vinegar?
A) No, because wine is non-mal.
B) Yes, and the debt remains unchanged.
C) Yes, and the debt reduces by the quantity lost in the process.
D) No, the pledge becomes void.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim pledgee may transform wine into vinegar to preserve the pledge, but only if the pledgor is Muslim; a dead animal skin cannot be pledged even after tanning.

intermediate

Rulings on Pledging Wine and Its Transformation
Expounds Hanafi rulings on wine/vinegar pledges with qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings for pledge (rahn) contracts where wine or juice changes state, distinguishing the effects based on the religion of the parties.

Core Idea
The validity of a wine pledge hinges on whether wine is property for the pledger; transformation to vinegar can salvage the contract only under specific conditions.

In Hanafi fiqh, wine (khamr) is not property for Muslims but is property for dhimmis. This distinction leads to nuanced rulings when wine is pledged or when juice turns into wine. This lesson examines the key cases from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, including the mortgagee's options when wine turns into vinegar, the invalidity of pledges from Muslims to non-Muslims, and the permissibility of turning fermented juice into vinegar to preserve the pledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and original substance
Determine the religion of the pledger and mortgagee, and what was originally pledged (wine, juice, etc.). This sets the framework for the ruling.
The religion of the pledger determines whether wine is considered property.
2
Assess the legal status of the substance
Is the substance mal (property) for the pledger? Wine is mal for dhimmis, not for Muslims. Juice is always mal.
This is the key differentiator.
3
Apply the general ruling on pledge validity
If the pledger's substance is not mal, the pledge is void; if it is mal, the pledge is valid initially.
For wine, the pledge is valid only if the pledger is non-Muslim.
4
Analyze transformation scenarios
If wine turns to vinegar, or juice to wine, additional rules apply: the mortgagee may have options, compensation may be due, and the pledge may continue or end depending on the parties' religions.
The text details four distinct cases.
5
Consider the principles of harm and qiyas
The rulings are shaped by preventing harm (darar) and riba, and analogized to cases of usurpation (ghasb) where a Muslim wrongfully takes wine from a dhimmi.
This analogy supports the choice given to the original owner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ارتهن المسلم من كافر خمراً فصار خلاً في الرهن باطل ويكون الخل أمانة في يده للراهن وهو بالخيار إن شاء أخذه قضاء دينه، وإن شاء يدع الخل بدينه إن كانت قيمة الخل يوم الرهن كالدين لأن المسلم يجوز أن يضمن الخمر بالرهن لأنه ضمان والمضمون متى نصب في يد الضمين يخير من له الضمان كما لو غصب المسلم خمراً من ذمي فصارت خلاً في يده يخير الذمي لأن الخمر عند أهل الذمة يصلح لمنافع ما لا يصلح له الخل ولا وجه فصار الخمر كالهالك من وجه، وليس له المرتهن خمراً مثل خمره لأن المسلم منهي عن تمليك الخمر ولا وجه أن يترك الخل عليه ويضمن النقصان لأنه يؤدي إلى الربا ولا وجه أن يأخذ الخل ويضمن الدين كله لأنه يتضرر به فقلنا بأنه يجعله بالدين ليدفع الضرر عنه وليس فيه ضرر على المرتهن فكذا هذا، فإذا وجبت قيمة الخمر للراهن على المرتهن فله عليه مثل ذلك فيلتقيان قصاصاً. ولو ارتهن الكافر خمراً من مسلم لا يجوز ويكون أمانة في يد المرتهن لأن الخمر لا يصير مضموناً على الكافر المسلم وإن قبضها بجهة الضمان كما في الغصب والإتلاف. ارتهن مسلم من مسلم عصيراً فصار خمراً فللمرتهن تخليلها وتكون رهناً، ويبطل من الدين بحساب ما نقص يعني من الكيل والوزن بقذف الزبد لأن التخليل إحياء حق المرتهن وإصلاح الفاسد فله ذلك، وفي إبقاء العقد بعد التخمير فائدة لجواز التخليل فيبقى كالعصير إذا تخمر قبل القبض يبقى البيع فكذا هذا، والدين يسقط بانتقاص الرهن لأنه احتبس عنده بعض الرهن ولا ينقص بانتقاص القيمة كما إذا تغير السعر وقيدنا بذكر العصير في المسلم قال وإن كان الراهن كافراً يأخذ الخمر والدين عليه وليس للمرتهن أن يخلها، فإن خللها ضمن قيمتها يوم خلل ورجع بدينه بخلاف ما لو كان الراهن مسلماً فخللها لم يضمن. والفرق أن هناك لا ضرر على الراهن في إبقاء عقد الرهن إلى ما بعد التخمير بل له فيه منفعة لأن ماله يصير متقوماً بالتخليل ولم يصر المرتهن متلفاً لماله بل إضرار بالراهن لأن لأهل رغائب في الخمر ما ليس مثلها في العصير وهو لم يرضى بكون الخمر رهناً، فلو بقينا عقد الرهن بعد تغيير ملكه في حقه يؤدي إلى الضرر به لأن الخمر بالعصير جنسان مختلفان في حق أهل الذمة وهو لم يعقد الرهن على الخمر وإنما عقد على العصير فلا يبقى العقد فيكون للراهن أخذ الخمر من المرتهن، فإن خللها يضمن قيمتها لأنه أتلف الخمر بالتخليل على الذمي لما بينا والله أعلم.
He said: If a Muslim takes wine as pledge from a non-Muslim and it becomes vinegar while in pledge, the pledge is void and the vinegar is a trust in his hand for the pledger. The pledger has a choice: if he wishes, he takes it as payment of his debt; if he wishes, he leaves the vinegar for his debt, provided the value of the vinegar at the time of pledge equals the debt. Because a Muslim may guarantee wine by pledge, as it is guarantee; whenever the guaranteed item is placed in the hand of the guarantor, the one entitled to the guarantee is given a choice, just as if a Muslim wrongfully takes wine from a dhimmi and it becomes vinegar in his hand, the dhimmi is given a choice. For wine with the dhimmis is suitable for some benefits for which vinegar is not suitable, and so wine is like destroyed in one respect. The mortgagee does not have the option to take wine like his wine because the Muslim is forbidden from owning wine. And there is no way to leave the vinegar upon him and guarantee the deficiency because it leads to riba. And there is no way to take the vinegar and guarantee the full debt because he would be harmed. So we said he makes it for the debt to remove harm from him, and there is no harm upon the mortgagee. Similarly, if the value of the wine becomes obligatory for the pledger upon the mortgagee, then the mortgagee has the like of that upon the pledger, so they offset each other. If a non-Muslim takes wine as pledge from a Muslim, it is not valid and it is a trust in the hand of the mortgagee, because wine does not become guaranteed upon a non-Muslim regarding a Muslim, even if he took it by way of guarantee, as in usurpation and destruction. If a Muslim takes juice as pledge from a Muslim and it becomes wine, the mortgagee may turn it into vinegar and it remains a pledge, and the debt is reduced by the amount of the deficiency, meaning in measure and weight by the casting off of foam, because turning into vinegar is reviving the right of the mortgagee and rectifying the corrupted, so he may do that. There is benefit in preserving the contract after fermentation for the permissibility of making vinegar, so it remains like juice if it ferments before taking possession, the sale remains. Similarly, the debt decreases by the reduction of the pledge because only part of the pledge is held, and it does not decrease by reduction in value as when the price changes. We restricted by mentioning juice for a Muslim. He said: If the pledger is a non-Muslim, he takes the wine and the debt remains upon him, and the mortgagee may not turn it into vinegar. If he turns it into vinegar, he guarantees its value on the day he turned it into vinegar and returns to his debt, unlike if the pledger was Muslim and he turned it into vinegar, he does not guarantee. The difference is that there is no harm upon the pledger in preserving the pledge contract after fermentation; rather there is benefit for him because his property becomes valuable by turning into vinegar, and the mortgagee did not destroy his property. But in the case of the non-Muslim pledger, it is harmful because the people have desires for wine that are not like those for juice, and he did not consent to the wine being a pledge. If we preserved the pledge contract after the change of his property in his right, it would lead to harm because wine and juice are two different types in the right of the dhimmis, and he did not contract the pledge on wine but on juice, so the contract does not remain. Thus the pledger takes the wine from the mortgagee. If he turns it into vinegar, he guarantees its value because he destroyed the wine by making vinegar upon the dhimmi, as we explained. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage is a set of rulings from the Hanafi school regarding pledges (رهن) involving wine (خمر) and vinegar (خل) or juice (عصير). The commentator expands on cases where the pledged item changes substance. The key principle is that wine is not a property that can be guaranteed for Muslims, but for non-Muslims it is. When wine turns into vinegar, the ruling depends on the religion of the parties and the original state. If a Muslim pledges wine from a non-Muslim, the pledge becomes void because the Muslim cannot own wine, and the vinegar becomes a trust; the pledger may take it as payment or leave it. If a non-Muslim pledges wine from a Muslim, it is invalid. If a Muslim pledges juice from another Muslim and it becomes wine, the mortgagee may turn it into vinegar and the pledge continues, with the debt reduced proportionally. If a non-Muslim pledges juice and it becomes wine, the mortgagee cannot turn it into vinegar; if he does, he must compensate the pledger. The reasoning is based on analogy (qiyas) to cases of usurpation (غصب) and the principle of avoiding harm (ضرر) and riba. The authoritative weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah as it appears in the commentary.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Wine, an intoxicating beverage; in this context, a non-property for Muslims but property for non-Muslims.
خل (khall): Vinegar, the result of wine turning sour; considered a pure and valuable substance.
رهن (rahn): Pledge/pawn; a contract where property is held as security for a debt.
أمانة (amanah): Trust; property held without liability for loss except by negligence.
تخليل (takhleel): The process of turning wine into vinegar intentionally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a non-Muslim mortgages juice to a Muslim, and the juice becomes wine, what is the ruling?
A) The mortgagee may turn it into vinegar, and the pledge continues.
B) The mortgagee cannot turn it into vinegar; if he does, he compensates the value.
C) The pledge becomes void immediately.
D) The mortgagee must sell the wine and keep the proceeds.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of a wine pledge and the permissibility of transforming it into vinegar depend critically on whether the pledger is Muslim or non-Muslim, based on the concept of mal and the principle of preventing harm.
